         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;">
         <a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">
          Glottolog
         </a>
        </th>

         </code>

         </span>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glottolog_9-0">

          </a>
         </sup>

         </span>

         </sup>
        </td>

       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;">
         <a href="/wiki/Linguasphere_Observatory" title="Linguasphere Observatory">
          Linguasphere
         </a>
        </th>
        <td style="line-height:1.35em;">
         <div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsible-leftside-toggle mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;">
          <div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
           <code>
            further information
           </code>
          </div>
          <div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
           <code>
            52-AC (Continental West Germanic)
            <br/>
            &gt; 52-ACB (Deutsch &amp; Dutch)
            <br/>
            &gt; 52-ACB-d (
            <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">
             Central German
            </a>
            incl. 52-ACB–dl &amp; -dm
            <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">
             Standard/Generalised High German
            </a>
            )
            <br/>
            + 52-ACB-e &amp; -f (
            <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">
             Upper German
            </a>
            &amp;
            <a href="/wiki/Swiss_German" title="Swiss German">
             Swiss German
            </a>
            )
            <br/>
            + 52-ACB-h (
            <a href="/wiki/German_diaspora" title="German diaspora">
             émigré German
            </a>
            varieties incl. 52-ACB-hc
            <a href="/wiki/Hutterite_German" title="Hutterite German">

            </a>
            &amp; 52-ACB-he
            <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_German_language" title="Pennsylvania German language">
             Pennsylvania German
            </a>
            etc.)
            <br/>
            + 52-ACB-i (
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yeniche_language" title="Yeniche language">
             Yenish
            </a>
            );
            <br/>
            Totalling 285 varieties: 52-ACB-daa to 52-ACB-i
           </code>
          </div>

       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height:1.35em;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Legal_statuses_of_German_in_the_world.svg">
          <img alt="Legal statuses of German in the world.svg" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="1326" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Legal_statuses_of_German_in_the_world.svg/220px-Legal_statuses_of_German_in_the_world.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Legal_statuses_of_German_in_the_world.svg/330px-Legal_statuses_of_German_in_the_world.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Legal_statuses_of_German_in_the_world.svg/440px-Legal_statuses_of_German_in_the_world.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
         </a>
         <div style="text-align:left;">
          <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
           <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffcc00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
           </span>
           (Co-)Official and majority language
          </div>
          <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
           <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #d98575; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
           </span>
           Co-official, but not majority language
          </div>
          <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
           <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #7373d9; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
           </span>
           Statutory minority/cultural language
          </div>
          <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
           <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #30efe3; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
           </span>
           Non-statutory minority language
          </div>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="noprint selfref" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#ddddff;padding:0.3em 0.5em;text-align:left;line-height:1.3;">
         <b>
          This article contains
          <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
           IPA
          </a>
          phonetic symbols.
         </b>
         Without proper
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet#Fonts" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          rendering support
         </a>
         , you may see
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Replacement_character" title="Replacement character">
          question marks, boxes, or other symbols
         </a>
         instead of
         <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
          Unicode
         </a>
         characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
          Help:IPA
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        German
       </b>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <i>
          Deutsch
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">
         [dɔʏtʃ]
        </a>
       </span>
       <small class="nowrap">
        (
        <span class="unicode haudio">
         <span class="fn">
          <span style="white-space:nowrap">
           <a href="/wiki/File:De-Deutsch.ogg" title="About this sound">
            <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
           </a>
          </span>
          <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/De-Deutsch.ogg" title="De-Deutsch.ogg">
           listen
          </a>
         </span>
        </span>
        )
       </small>
       ) is a
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
        West Germanic language
       </a>
       that is mainly spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">
        Central Europe
       </a>
       . It is the most widely spoken and (co-) official language in
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
        Austria
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">
        Switzerland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/South_Tyrol" title="South Tyrol">
        South Tyrol
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       ), the
       <a href="/wiki/German-speaking_Community_of_Belgium" title="German-speaking Community of Belgium">
        German-speaking Community of Belgium
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">
        Liechtenstein
       </a>
       . It is also one of the three official languages of
       <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">
        Luxembourg
       </a>
       . The languages which are most similar to German are the other members of the West Germanic language branch:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afrikaans_language" title="Afrikaans language">
        Afrikaans
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">
        Dutch
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Frisian_languages" title="Frisian languages">
        Frisian languages
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low German/Low Saxon
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luxembourgish_language" title="Luxembourgish language">
        Luxembourgish
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       . German is the second most widely spoken
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic language
       </a>
       , after English.
      </p>
      <p>
       One of the
       <a href="/wiki/World_language" title="World language">
        major languages of the world
       </a>
       , German is the first language of about 95 million people worldwide and the most widely spoken native language in the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       German also is the fourth most widely taught non-English language in the US
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLA_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MLA-12">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (after
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language_in_the_United_States" title="Spanish language in the United States">
        Spanish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/French_language_in_the_United_States#Language_study" title="French language in the United States">
        French
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/American_Sign_Language" title="American Sign Language">
        American Sign Language
       </a>
       ) and third in the EU (after English and French; at lower secondary level),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the second most commonly used scientific language
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goethe1_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-goethe1-14">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as well as the fourth most widely used language on websites (after
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">
        Russian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">
        Japanese
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German-speaking_countries" title="German-speaking countries">
        German-speaking countries
       </a>
       are ranked fifth in terms of annual publication of new books, with one tenth of all books (including e-books) in the world being published in the German language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdsu_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sdsu-16">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       German derives the majority of its vocabulary from the ancient Germanic branch of the
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European
       </a>
       language family. A portion of German words are derived from
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       , and fewer are borrowed from
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       . With slightly different standardized variants (
       <a href="/wiki/German_Standard_German" title="German Standard German">
        German
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austrian_Standard_German" title="Austrian Standard German">
        Austrian
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Standard_German" title="Swiss Standard German">
        Swiss Standard German
       </a>
       ), German is a
       <a href="/wiki/Pluricentric_language" title="Pluricentric language">
        pluricentric language
       </a>
       . Like English, German is also notable for its
       <a href="/wiki/German_dialects" title="German dialects">
        broad spectrum of dialects
       </a>
       , with many unique varieties existing in Europe and also other parts of the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Due to the limited intelligibility between certain varieties and
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">
        Standard German
       </a>
       , as well as the lack of an undisputed, scientific difference between a "dialect" and a "language",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       some German varieties or dialect groups (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low German
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Plautdietsch_language" title="Plautdietsch language">
        Plautdietsch
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goossens2000_5-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Goossens2000-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) are alternatively referred to as "languages" and "dialects".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_(2015)_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue_(2015)-18">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31">

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
        Classification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Modern
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">
        Standard German
       </a>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
        West Germanic language
       </a>
       descended from the
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic
       </a>
       branch of the
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European languages
       </a>
       . The Germanic languages are traditionally subdivided into three branches:
       <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
        North Germanic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/East_Germanic_languages" title="East Germanic languages">
        East Germanic
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
        West Germanic
       </a>
       . The first of these branches survives in modern
       <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">
        Danish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">
        Swedish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">
        Norwegian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">
        Faroese
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">
        Icelandic
       </a>
       , all of which are descended from
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">
        Old Norse
       </a>
       . The East Germanic languages are now extinct, and the only historical member of this branch from which written texts survive is
       <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">
        Gothic
       </a>
       . The West Germanic languages, however, have undergone extensive dialectal subdivision and are now represented in modern languages such as
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       , German,
       <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">
        Dutch
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">
        Afrikaans
       </a>
       , and others.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-19">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Germanic_Languages_Map_Europe.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="911" data-file-width="1150" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Germanic_Languages_Map_Europe.png/240px-Germanic_Languages_Map_Europe.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Germanic_Languages_Map_Europe.png/360px-Germanic_Languages_Map_Europe.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Germanic_Languages_Map_Europe.png/480px-Germanic_Languages_Map_Europe.png 2x" width="240"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Germanic_Languages_Map_Europe.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
          Germanic languages
         </a>
         in Europe
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Within the West Germanic language dialect continuum, the
       <a href="/wiki/Benrath_line" title="Benrath line">
        Benrath
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Uerdingen_line" title="Uerdingen line">
        Uerdingen
       </a>
       lines (running through
       <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">
        Düsseldorf
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf-Benrath" title="Düsseldorf-Benrath">
        Benrath
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Krefeld" title="Krefeld">
        Krefeld
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Uerdingen" title="Uerdingen">
        Uerdingen
       </a>
       , respectively) serve to distinguish the Germanic dialects that were affected by the
       <a href="/wiki/High_German_consonant_shift" title="High German consonant shift">
        High German consonant shift
       </a>
       (south of Benrath) from those that were not (north of Uerdingen). The various regional dialects spoken south of these lines are grouped as
       <a href="/wiki/High_German_languages" title="High German languages">
        High German
       </a>
       dialects
       <i>
        (nos. 29-34 on the map)
       </i>
       , while those spoken to the north comprise the
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low German
       </a>
       /Low Saxon
       <i>
        (nos. 19-24)
       </i>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Low_Franconian_languages" title="Low Franconian languages">
        Low Franconian
       </a>
       <i>
        (no. 25)
       </i>
       dialects. As members of the West Germanic language family, High German, Low German, and Low Franconian can be further distinguished historically as
       <a href="/wiki/Irminones" title="Irminones">
        Irminonic
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ingvaeonic_languages" title="Ingvaeonic languages">
        Ingvaeonic
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Istvaeones" title="Istvaeones">
        Istvaeonic
       </a>
       , respectively. This classification indicates their historical descent from dialects spoken by the Irminones (also known as the Elbe group), Ingvaeones (or North Sea Germanic group), and Istvaeones (or Weser-Rhine group).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_19-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-19">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">
        Standard German
       </a>
       is based on
       <a href="/wiki/Thuringian_dialect" title="Thuringian dialect">
        Thuringian
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Saxon_German" title="Upper Saxon German">
        Upper Saxon
       </a>
       dialects
       <i>
        (no. 30 on the map)
       </i>
       , which are
       <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">
        Central German
       </a>
       dialects
       <i>
        (nos. 29-31)
       </i>
       , belonging to the Irminonic
       <a href="/wiki/High_German_languages" title="High German languages">
        High German
       </a>
       dialect group. German is therefore most closely related to the other languages based on High German dialects, such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luxembourgish_language" title="Luxembourgish language">
        Luxembourgish
       </a>
       (based on
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Franconian_dialects" title="Central Franconian dialects">
        Central Franconian dialects
       </a>
       –
       <i>
        no. 29
       </i>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       . Also closely related to Standard German are the
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">
        Upper German
       </a>
       dialects spoken in the southern
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_territorial_entities_where_German_is_an_official_language" title="List of territorial entities where German is an official language">
        German-speaking countries
       </a>
       , such as
       <a href="/wiki/Swiss_German" title="Swiss German">
        Swiss German
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">
        Alemannic dialects
       </a>
       –
       <i>
        no. 34
       </i>
       ), and the various dialects spoken in the
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        French
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_France" title="Regions of France">
        region
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Grand_Est" title="Grand Est">
        Grand Est
       </a>
       , such as
       <a href="/wiki/Alsatian_dialect" title="Alsatian dialect">
        Alsatian
       </a>
       (mainly Alemannic, but also Central- and
       <a href="/wiki/High_Franconian_German" title="High Franconian German">
        Upper Franconian
       </a>
       <i>
        (no. 32)
       </i>
       dialects) and
       <a href="/wiki/Lorraine_Franconian" title="Lorraine Franconian">
        Lorraine Franconian
       </a>
       (Central Franconian –
       <i>
        no. 29
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       After these High German dialects, standard German is (somewhat less closely) related to languages based on Low Franconian dialects (e.g. Dutch and Afrikaans) or Low German/Low Saxon dialects (spoken in northern
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       and southern
       <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">
        Denmark
       </a>
       ), neither of which underwent the High German consonant shift. As has been noted, the former of these dialect types is Istvaeonic and the latter Ingvaeonic, whereas the High German dialects are all Irminonic; the differences between these languages and standard German are therefore considerable. Also related to German are the Frisian languages—
       <a href="/wiki/North_Frisian_language" title="North Frisian language">
        North Frisian
       </a>
       (spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">
        Schleswig-Holstein
       </a>
       <i>
        no. 28
       </i>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Saterland_Frisian_language" title="Saterland Frisian language">
        Saterland Frisian
       </a>
       (spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxony" title="Lower Saxony">
        Lower Saxony
       </a>
       –
       <i>
        no. 27
       </i>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/West_Frisian_language" title="West Frisian language">
        West Frisian
       </a>
       (spoken in the
       <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">
        Netherlands
       </a>
       –
       <i>
        no. 26
       </i>
       )—as well as the Anglic languages of
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">
        Scots
       </a>
       . These
       <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian_languages" title="Anglo-Frisian languages">
        Anglo-Frisian
       </a>
       dialects are all members of the Ingvaeonic family of West Germanic languages which did not take part in the High German consonant shift.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_German" title="History of German">
        History of German
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Old_High_German">
        Old High German
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Old High German">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">
        Old High German
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_German" title="History of German">
        history of the German language
       </a>
       begins with the
       <a href="/wiki/High_German_consonant_shift" title="High German consonant shift">
        High German consonant shift
       </a>
       during the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Migration_period" title="Migration period">
        migration period
       </a>
       , which separated
       <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">
        Old High German
       </a>
       (OHG) dialects from
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">
        Old Saxon
       </a>
       . This
       <a href="/wiki/Sound_change" title="Sound change">
        sound shift
       </a>
       involved a drastic change in the pronunciation of both
       <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">
        voiced
       </a>
       and voiceless
       <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">
        stop consonants
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        d
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        g
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        p
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       , respectively). The primary effects of the shift were the following:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Voiceless stops became long (
        <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">
         geminated
        </a>
        ) voiceless
        <a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">
         fricatives
        </a>
        following a vowel
       </li>
       <li>
        Voiceless stops became
        <a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" title="Affricate consonant">
         affricates
        </a>
        in word-initial position, or following certain consonants
       </li>
       <li>
        Voiced stops became voiceless in certain phonetic settings.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_20-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-:02-20">
          [17]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Voiceless stop
         <br/>
         following a vowel
        </th>
        <th>
         Word-initial
         <br/>
         voiceless stop
        </th>
        <th>
         Voiced stop
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         /p/→/ff/
        </td>
        <td>
         /p/→/pf/
        </td>
        <td>
         /b/→/p/
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         /t/→/ss/
        </td>
        <td>
         /t/→/ts/
        </td>
        <td>
         /d/→/t/
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         /k/→/xx/
        </td>
        <td>
         /k/→/kx/
        </td>
        <td>
         /g/→/k/
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       While there is written evidence of the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">
        Old High German
       </a>
       language in several
       <a href="/wiki/Elder_Futhark" title="Elder Futhark">
        Elder Futhark
       </a>
       inscriptions from as early as the 6th century AD (such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Pforzen_buckle" title="Pforzen buckle">
        Pforzen buckle
       </a>
       ), the Old High German period is generally seen as beginning with the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Abrogans" title="Abrogans">
         Abrogans
        </a>
       </i>
       (written c.765-775), a Latin-German
       <a href="/wiki/Glossary" title="Glossary">
        glossary
       </a>
       supplying over 3,000 OHG words with their
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       equivalents. Following the
       <i>
        Abrogans
       </i>
       the first coherent works written in OHG appear in the 9th century, chief among them being the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Muspilli" title="Muspilli">
         Muspilli
        </a>
       </i>
       , the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merseburg_Incantations" title="Merseburg Incantations">
         Merseberg Incantations
        </a>
       </i>
       , and the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Hildebrandslied" title="Hildebrandslied">
         Hildebrandslied
        </a>
       </i>
       , as well as a number of other religious texts (the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Georgslied" title="Georgslied">
         Georgslied
        </a>
       </i>
       , the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ludwigslied" title="Ludwigslied">
         Ludwigslied
        </a>
       </i>
       , the
       <i>
        Evangelienbuch
       </i>
       , and translated hymns and prayers).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:02-20">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calvin2_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Calvin2-21">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <i>
        Muspilli
       </i>
       is a Christian poem written in a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bavarian_dialects" title="Bavarian dialects">
        Bavarian
       </a>
       dialect offering an account of the soul after the
       <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">
        Last Judgment
       </a>
       , and the
       <i>
        Merseberg Incantations
       </i>
       are transcriptions of spells and charms from the
       <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">
        pagan
       </a>
       Germanic tradition. Of particular interest to scholars, however, has been the
       <i>
        Hildebrandslied
       </i>
       , a secular
       <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">
        epic poem
       </a>
       telling the tale of an estranged father and son unknowingly meeting each other in battle. Linguistically this text is highly interesting due to the mixed use of
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">
        Old Saxon
       </a>
       and Old High German dialects in its composition. The written works of this period stem mainly from the
       <a href="/wiki/Alemanni" title="Alemanni">
        Alamanni
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bavarian_dialects" title="Bavarian dialects">
        Bavarian
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Thuringian_dialect" title="Thuringian dialect">
        Thuringian
       </a>
       groups, all belonging to the Elbe Germanic group (
       <a href="/wiki/Irminones" title="Irminones">
        Irminones
       </a>
       ), which had settled in what is now southern-central
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
        Austria
       </a>
       between the 2nd and 6th centuries during the great migration.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_20-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:02-20">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In general, the surviving texts of OHG show a wide range of
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">
        dialectal
       </a>
       diversity with very little written uniformity. The early written tradition of OHG survived mostly through
       <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">
        monasteries
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Scriptorium" title="Scriptorium">
        scriptoria
       </a>
       as local translations of Latin originals; as a result, the surviving texts are written in highly disparate regional dialects and exhibit significant Latin influence, particularly in vocabulary.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_20-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:02-20">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At this point monasteries, where most written works were produced, were dominated by Latin, and German saw only occasional use in official and ecclesiastical writing.
      </p>
      <p>
       The German language through the OHG period was still predominantly a spoken language, with a wide range of dialects and a much more extensive
       <a href="/wiki/Oral_literature" title="Oral literature">
        oral
       </a>
       tradition than a written one. Having just emerged from the High German consonant shift, OHG was also a relatively new and volatile language still undergoing a number of
       <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">
        phonetic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">
        phonological
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">
        morphological
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">
        syntactic
       </a>
       changes. The scarcity of written work, instability of the language, and widespread illiteracy of the time thus account for the lack of
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">
        standardization
       </a>
       up to the end of the OHG period in 1050.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_High_German">
        Middle High German
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Middle High German">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlthochdeutscheSprachr%C3%A4ume962_Box.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="874" data-file-width="800" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/AlthochdeutscheSprachr%C3%A4ume962_Box.jpg/170px-AlthochdeutscheSprachr%C3%A4ume962_Box.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/AlthochdeutscheSprachr%C3%A4ume962_Box.jpg/255px-AlthochdeutscheSprachr%C3%A4ume962_Box.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/AlthochdeutscheSprachr%C3%A4ume962_Box.jpg/340px-AlthochdeutscheSprachr%C3%A4ume962_Box.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlthochdeutscheSprachr%C3%A4ume962_Box.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Germanic-speaking area of the
         <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">
          Holy Roman Empire
         </a>
         around AD 962.
         <hr/>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #B298C4; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Old Frisian (
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Alt-Friesisch
           </span>
          </i>
          )
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #DB9564; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Old Saxon (
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Alt-Sächsisch
           </span>
          </i>
          )
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FE8741; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Old Franconian (
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Alt-Fränkisch
           </span>
          </i>
          )
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FCC54B; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Old Alemannic (
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Alt-Alemannisch
           </span>
          </i>
          )
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FE9A2C; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Old Bavarian (
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Alt-Bairisch
           </span>
          </i>
          )
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_High_German" title="Middle High German">
        Middle High German
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       While there is no complete agreement over the dates of the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_High_German" title="Middle High German">
        Middle High German
       </a>
       (MHG) period, it is generally seen as lasting from 1050 to 1350.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:2-22">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-23">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was a period of significant expansion of the geographical territory occupied by Germanic tribes, and consequently of the number of German speakers. Whereas during the Old High German period the Germanic tribes extended only as far east as the
       <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">
        Elbe
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Saale" title="Saale">
        Saale
       </a>
       rivers, the MHG period saw a number of these tribes expanding beyond this eastern boundary into
       <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">
        Slavic
       </a>
       territory (this is known as the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ostsiedlung" title="Ostsiedlung">
         Ostsiedlung
        </a>
       </i>
       ). Along with the increasing wealth and geographic extent of the Germanic groups came greater use of German in the courts of nobles as the standard language of official proceedings and literature.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-23">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A clear example of this is the
       <i>
        mittelhochdeutsche Dichtersprache
       </i>
       employed in the
       <a href="/wiki/Hohenstaufen" title="Hohenstaufen">
        Hohenstaufen
       </a>
       court in
       <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">
        Swabia
       </a>
       as a standardized supra-dialectal written language. While these efforts were still regionally bound, German began to be used in place of Latin for certain official purposes, leading to a greater need for regularity in written conventions.
      </p>
      <p>
       While the major changes of the MHG period were socio-cultural, German was still undergoing significant linguistic changes in syntax, phonetics, and morphology as well (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_breaking" title="Vowel breaking">
        diphthongization
       </a>
       of certain vowel sounds:
       <i>
        hus
       </i>
       (OHG "house")
       <i>
        →haus
       </i>
       (MHG), and weakening of unstressed short vowels to
       <a href="/wiki/Schwa" title="Schwa">
        schwa
       </a>
       [ə]:
       <i>
        taga
       </i>
       (OHG "days")→
       <i>
        tage
       </i>
       (MHG)).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A great wealth of texts survives from the MHG period. Significantly, among this repertoire are a number of impressive secular works, such as the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nibelungenlied" title="Nibelungenlied">
         Nibelungenlied
        </a>
       </i>
       , an
       <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">
        epic poem
       </a>
       telling the story of the
       <a href="/wiki/Dragon" title="Dragon">
        dragon
       </a>
       -slayer
       <a href="/wiki/Sigurd" title="Sigurd">
        Siegfried
       </a>
       (c. 13th century), and the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Iwein" title="Iwein">
         Iwein
        </a>
        ,
       </i>
       an
       <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">
        Arthurian
       </a>
       verse poem by
       <a href="/wiki/Hartmann_von_Aue" title="Hartmann von Aue">
        Hartmann von Aue
       </a>
       (c. 1203), as well as several
       <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">
        lyric poems
       </a>
       and courtly romances such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Parzival" title="Parzival">
         Parzival
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tristan" title="Tristan">
         Tristan
        </a>
       </i>
       . (Also noteworthy is the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sachsenspiegel" title="Sachsenspiegel">
         Sachsenspiegel
        </a>
       </i>
       , the first book of laws written in
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Low_German" title="Middle Low German">
        Middle
        <i>
         Low
        </i>
        German
       </a>
       (c. 1220)). The abundance and (secular) character of the literature of the MHG period demonstrate the beginnings of a standardized written form of German, as well as the desire of poets and authors to be understood by individuals on supra-dialectal terms.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Middle High German period is generally seen as ending with the decimation of the population of
       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
        Europe
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">
        Black Death
       </a>
       of 1346–1353.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_22-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:2-22">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_New_High_German">
        Early New High German
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early New High German">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Early_New_High_German" title="Early New High German">
        Early New High German
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deutsch-Niederl%C3%A4ndischer_Sprachraum_(nach_Werner_K%C3%B6nig).png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2232" data-file-width="2912" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Deutsch-Niederl%C3%A4ndischer_Sprachraum_%28nach_Werner_K%C3%B6nig%29.png/300px-Deutsch-Niederl%C3%A4ndischer_Sprachraum_%28nach_Werner_K%C3%B6nig%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Deutsch-Niederl%C3%A4ndischer_Sprachraum_%28nach_Werner_K%C3%B6nig%29.png/450px-Deutsch-Niederl%C3%A4ndischer_Sprachraum_%28nach_Werner_K%C3%B6nig%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Deutsch-Niederl%C3%A4ndischer_Sprachraum_%28nach_Werner_K%C3%B6nig%29.png/600px-Deutsch-Niederl%C3%A4ndischer_Sprachraum_%28nach_Werner_K%C3%B6nig%29.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deutsch-Niederl%C3%A4ndischer_Sprachraum_(nach_Werner_K%C3%B6nig).png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         German-Dutch language area before and after the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_and_expulsion_of_Germans_(1944-1950)" title="Flight and expulsion of Germans (1944-1950)">
          flight and expulsion of Germans (1944-1950)
         </a>
         from much of eastern and central Europe. Areas in the east where German is no longer spoken are marked by lighter shades.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Modern German begins with the
       <a href="/wiki/Early_New_High_German" title="Early New High German">
        Early New High German
       </a>
       (ENHG) period, which the influential German
       <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">
        philologist
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Scherer" title="Wilhelm Scherer">
        Wilhelm Scherer
       </a>
       dates 1350–1650, terminating with the end of the
       <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">
        Thirty Years' War
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_22-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:2-22">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This period saw the further displacement of Latin by German as the primary language of courtly proceedings and, increasingly, of literature in the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_in_the_Holy_Roman_Empire" title="List of states in the Holy Roman Empire">
        German states
       </a>
       . While these states were still under the control of the
       <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">
        Holy Roman Empire
       </a>
       and far from any form of unification, the desire for a cohesive written language that would be understandable across the many German-speaking
       <a href="/wiki/Principality" title="Principality">
        principalities
       </a>
       and kingdoms was stronger than ever. As a spoken language German remained highly fractured through this period with a vast number of often mutually-incomprehensible
       <a href="/wiki/German_dialects" title="German dialects">
        regional dialects
       </a>
       being spoken throughout the German states; the invention of the
       <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">
        printing press
       </a>
       c.1440 and the publication of
       <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">
        Luther's vernacular translation of the Bible
       </a>
       in 1534, however, had an immense effect on standardizing German as a supra-dialectal written language.
      </p>
      <p>
       The ENHG period saw the rise of several important cross-regional forms of
       <a href="/wiki/Chancery_(medieval_office)" title="Chancery (medieval office)">
        chancery
       </a>
       German, one being
       <i>
        gemeine tiutsch,
       </i>
       used in the court of the
       <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">
        Holy Roman Emperor
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Maximilian I, Holy Roman Emperor">
        Maximilian I
       </a>
       , and the other being
       <i>
        Meißner Deutsch
       </i>
       , used in the
       <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Saxony" title="Electorate of Saxony">
        Electorate of Saxony
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Saxe-Wittenberg" title="Duchy of Saxe-Wittenberg">
        Duchy of Saxe-Wittenberg
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Alongside these courtly written standards, the invention of the printing press led to the development of a number of printers' languages (
       <i>
        Druckersprachen
       </i>
       ) aimed at making printed material readable and understandable across as many diverse dialects of German as possible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The greater ease of production and increased availability of written texts brought about increased standardization in the written form of the German language.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lutherbibel.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="759" data-file-width="1000" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/220px-Lutherbibel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/330px-Lutherbibel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/440px-Lutherbibel.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lutherbibel.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The widespread popularity of the
         <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">
          Bible
         </a>
         translated into German by
         <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">
          Martin Luther
         </a>
         helped establish modern German
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       One of the central events in the development of ENHG was the publication of Luther's translation of the Bible into German (the
       <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
        New Testament
       </a>
       in 1522 and the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
        Old Testament
       </a>
       , published in parts and completed in 1534). Luther based his translation primarily on the
       <i>
        Meißner Deutsch
       </i>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">
        Saxony
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       spending much time among the population of Saxony researching the dialect so as to make the work as natural and accessible to German speakers as possible. Copies of Luther's Bible featured a long list of
       <a href="/wiki/Gloss_(annotation)" title="Gloss (annotation)">
        glosses
       </a>
       for each region that translated words which were unknown in the region into the regional dialect. Concerning his translation method Luther says the following:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        One who would talk German does not ask the Latin how he shall do it; he must ask the mother in the home, the children on the streets, the common man in the market-place and note carefully how they talk, then translate accordingly. They will then understand what is said to them because it is German. When Christ says 'ex abundantia cordis os loquitur,' I would translate, if I followed the papists,
        <i>
         aus dem Überflusz des Herzens redet der Mund
        </i>
        . But tell me is this talking German? What German understands such stuff? No, the mother in the home and the plain man would say,
        <i>
         Wesz das Herz voll ist, des gehet der Mund über
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
         <a href="#cite_note-29">
          [26]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       With Luther's rendering of the Bible in the vernacular German asserted itself against the dominance of Latin as a legitimate language for courtly, literary, and now ecclesiastical subject-matter. Further, his Bible was ubiquitous in the German states with nearly every household possessing a copy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nevertheless, even with the influence of Luther's Bible as an unofficial written standard, it was not until the middle of the 18th century after the ENHG period that a widely accepted standard for written German appeared.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Austrian_Empire">
        Austrian Empire
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Austrian Empire">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Austria_Hungary_ethnic.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="1360" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Austria_Hungary_ethnic.svg/220px-Austria_Hungary_ethnic.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Austria_Hungary_ethnic.svg/330px-Austria_Hungary_ethnic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Austria_Hungary_ethnic.svg/440px-Austria_Hungary_ethnic.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Austria_Hungary_ethnic.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Ethnolinguistic map of
         <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">
          Austria-Hungary
         </a>
         , 1910, with German-speaking areas shown in red
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       German was the language of commerce and government in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habsburg_Empire" title="Habsburg Empire">
        Habsburg Empire
       </a>
       , which encompassed a large area of Central and Eastern Europe. Until the mid-19th century, it was essentially the language of townspeople throughout most of the Empire. Its use indicated that the speaker was a
       <a href="/wiki/Merchant" title="Merchant">
        merchant
       </a>
       or someone from an urban area, regardless of nationality.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some cities, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">
        Prague
       </a>
       (German:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Prag
        </span>
       </i>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">
        Budapest
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Buda" title="Buda">
        Buda
       </a>
       , German:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Ofen
        </span>
       </i>
       ), were gradually
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanization" title="Germanization">
        Germanized
       </a>
       in the years after their incorporation into the Habsburg domain. Others, such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pozsony" title="Pozsony">
        Pozsony
       </a>
       (German:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Pressburg
        </span>
       </i>
       , now
       <a href="/wiki/Bratislava" title="Bratislava">
        Bratislava
       </a>
       ), were originally settled during the Habsburg period, and were primarily German at that time. Prague, Budapest and Bratislava as well as cities like
       <a href="/wiki/Zagreb" title="Zagreb">
        Zagreb
       </a>
       (German:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Agram
        </span>
       </i>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Ljubljana" title="Ljubljana">
        Ljubljana
       </a>
       (German:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Laibach
        </span>
       </i>
       ), contained significant German minorities.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the eastern provinces of
       <a href="/wiki/Banat" title="Banat">
        Banat
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">
        Transylvania
       </a>
       (German:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Siebenbürgen
        </span>
       </i>
       ), German was the predominant language not only in the larger towns – such as
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Temeswar
        </span>
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Timi%C8%99oara" title="Timișoara">
        Timișoara
       </a>
       ),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Hermannstadt
        </span>
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Sibiu" title="Sibiu">
        Sibiu
       </a>
       ) and
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Kronstadt
        </span>
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Bra%C8%99ov" title="Brașov">
        Brașov
       </a>
       ) – but also in many smaller localities in the surrounding areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Standardization">
        Standardization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Standardization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:German_dictionary.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="526" data-file-width="370" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/German_dictionary.jpg/220px-German_dictionary.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/German_dictionary.jpg/330px-German_dictionary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/50/German_dictionary.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:German_dictionary.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Deutsches_W%C3%B6rterbuch" title="Deutsches Wörterbuch">
           Deutsches Wörterbuch
          </a>
         </i>
         (1854) by the
         <a href="/wiki/Brothers_Grimm" title="Brothers Grimm">
          Brothers Grimm
         </a>
         helped to standardize German orthography.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The most comprehensive guide to the vocabulary of the German language is found within the
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a href="/wiki/Deutsches_W%C3%B6rterbuch" title="Deutsches Wörterbuch">
          Deutsches Wörterbuch
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       . This
       <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">
        dictionary
       </a>
       was created by the
       <a href="/wiki/Brothers_Grimm" title="Brothers Grimm">
        Brothers Grimm
       </a>
       and is composed of 16 parts which were issued between 1852 and 1860.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBAWsynopsis_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BBAWsynopsis-34">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1872, grammatical and orthographic rules first appeared in the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duden_Handbook" title="Duden Handbook">
         Duden Handbook
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1901, the 2nd Orthographical Conference ended with a complete
       <a href="/wiki/Standardization" title="Standardization">
        standardization
       </a>
       of the German language in its written form and the
       <i>
        Duden Handbook
       </i>
       was declared its standard definition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Deutsche Bühnensprache
        </span>
       </i>
       (literally, German
       <a href="/wiki/Stage_(theatre)" title="Stage (theatre)">
        stage
       </a>
       language) had established conventions for German pronunciation in theatre (
       <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BChnendeutsch" title="Bühnendeutsch">
        Bühnendeutsch
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) three years earlier; however, this was an artificial standard that did not correspond to any traditional spoken dialect. Rather, it was based on the pronunciation of Standard German in Northern Germany, although it was subsequently regarded often as a general prescriptive norm, despite differing pronunciation traditions especially in the Upper-German-speaking regions that still characterize the dialect of the area today – especially the pronunciation of the ending
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         -ig
        </span>
       </i>
       as [ɪk] instead of [ɪç]. In Northern Germany, Standard German was a foreign language to most inhabitants, whose native dialects were subsets of Low German. It was usually encountered only in writing or formal speech; in fact, most of Standard German was a written language, not identical to any spoken dialect, throughout the German-speaking area until well into the 19th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       Official revisions of some of the rules from 1901 were not issued until the controversial
       <a href="/wiki/German_orthography_reform_of_1996" title="German orthography reform of 1996">
        German orthography reform of 1996
       </a>
       was made the official standard by governments of all German-speaking countries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Upward_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Upward-38">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Media and written works are now almost all produced in Standard German (often called
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Hochdeutsch
        </span>
       </i>
       , "High German") which is understood in all areas where German is spoken.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">
        Geographic distribution
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Geographical_distribution_of_German_speakers" title="Geographical distribution of German speakers">
        Geographical distribution of German speakers
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright" style="">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:200px">
        <div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black">
         <div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:Grey">
         </div>
         <div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 202.51256191926px; border-left-color:#008751">
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#008751">
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#008751">
         </div>
         <div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 190.31049331139px; border-left-color:#FF0000">
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#FF0000">
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#FF0000">
         </div>
         <div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 90.420359373904px; border-left-color:Yellow">
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:Yellow">
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:Yellow">
         </div>
         <div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 21.036851205654px; border-left-color:Black">
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:Black">
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:Black">
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <p>
          Approximate distribution of native German speakers (assuming a rounded total of 95 million) worldwide.
         </p>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Black; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Germany (78.3%)
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Yellow; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Austria (8.4%)
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FF0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Switzerland (5.6%)
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #008751; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Italy (South Tyrol) (0.4%)
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Grey; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Other (7.3%)
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Due to the
       <a href="/wiki/German_diaspora" title="German diaspora">
        German diaspora
       </a>
       as well as German being the third most widely taught
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language" title="Foreign language">
        foreign language
       </a>
       in the US
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLA_12-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-MLA-12">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the EU
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurostat_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-eurostat-39">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       amongst others, the
       <a href="/wiki/Geographical_distribution_of_German_speakers" title="Geographical distribution of German speakers">
        geographical distribution of German speakers
       </a>
       (or "Germanophones") spans all inhabited continents. As for the number of speakers of any language worldwide, an assessment is always compromised by the lack of sufficient, reliable data. For an exact, global number of native German speakers, this is further complicated by the existence of several varieties whose status as separate "languages" or "dialects" is disputed for political and/or linguistic reasons, including quantitatively strong varieties like certain forms of
       <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">
        Alemannic
       </a>
       (e.g.,
       <a href="/wiki/Alsatian_dialect" title="Alsatian dialect">
        Alsatian
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low German
       </a>
       /Plautdietsch.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goossens2000_5-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Goossens2000-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mostly depending on the inclusion or exclusion of certain varieties, it is estimated that approximately 90–95 million people speak German as a
       <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">
        first language
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_(2015)_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue_(2015)-18">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marten_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marten-40">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       10-25 million as a
       <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">
        second language
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_(2015)_18-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue_(2015)-18">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 75–100 million as a
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language" title="Foreign language">
        foreign language
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurobarometer_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-eurobarometer-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This would imply approximately 175-220 million German speakers worldwide.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statista_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Statista-41">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is estimated that also including all persons who are or were taking German classes, i.e., regardless of their actual proficiency, would amount to about 280 million people worldwide with at least some knowledge of German.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <center>
       <table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; font-size:85%">

         <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">

         </a>
         outside Europe
         <br/>
         (according to Ethnologue 2016
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
          <a href="#cite_note-42">

          </a>
         </sup>
         unless referenced otherwise)
         <br/>

        </caption>

         </td>
         <td class="table-na" data-sort-value="" style="background: #ececec; color: #2C2C2C; vertical-align: middle; font-size: smaller; text-align: center;">

         </td>
         <td class="table-na" data-sort-value="" style="background: #ececec; color: #2C2C2C; vertical-align: middle; font-size: smaller; text-align: center;">
          N/A
         </td>
         <td class="table-na" data-sort-value="" style="background: #ececec; color: #2C2C2C; vertical-align: middle; font-size: smaller; text-align: center;">
          N/A
