      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Europe_and_Asia">
        Europe and Asia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Europe and Asia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Legal_statuses_of_German_in_Europe.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="552" data-file-width="769" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Legal_statuses_of_German_in_Europe.svg/450px-Legal_statuses_of_German_in_Europe.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Legal_statuses_of_German_in_Europe.svg/675px-Legal_statuses_of_German_in_Europe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Legal_statuses_of_German_in_Europe.svg/900px-Legal_statuses_of_German_in_Europe.svg.png 2x" width="450"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Legal_statuses_of_German_in_Europe.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The German language in Europe:
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <small>
           <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffcc00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
           </span>
           <b>
            "German
            <a href="/wiki/Sprachraum" title="Sprachraum">
             Sprachraum
            </a>
            "
           </b>
           : German is official language (de jure or de facto) and first language of the majority of the population
          </small>
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <small>
           <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #d98575; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
           </span>
           German is a co-official language, but not the first language of the majority of the population
          </small>
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <small>
           <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #7373d9; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
           </span>
           German (or a German dialect) is a legally recognized minority language (Squares: Geographic distribution too dispersed/small for map scale)
          </small>
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <small>
           <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #30efe3; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
           </span>
           German (or a variety of German) is spoken by a sizeable minority, but has no legal recognition
          </small>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="German_Sprachraum">
        German Sprachraum
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: German Sprachraum">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_territorial_entities_where_German_is_an_official_language" title="List of territorial entities where German is an official language">
        List of territorial entities where German is an official language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The area in central Europe where the majority of the population speaks German as a first language and has German as a (co-)official language is called the "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Sprachraum" title="German Sprachraum">
        German Sprachraum
       </a>
       ". It comprises an estimated 88 million native speakers and 10 million who speak German as a second language (e.g. immigrants).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_(2015)_18-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue_(2015)-18">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Excluding regional minority languages, German is the only official language of
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
         Germany
        </a>
        (de facto, not specified in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grundgesetz" title="Grundgesetz">
         constitution
        </a>
        ),
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
         Austria
        </a>
        (de jure),
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Switzerland" title="Languages of Switzerland">
         17 cantons of Switzerland
        </a>
        (de jure), and
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">
         Liechtenstein
        </a>
        (de jure).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       It is a co-official language of the
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
         Italian
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/South_Tyrol" title="South Tyrol">
         Autonomous Province of South Tyrol
        </a>
        (also majority language),
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">
         Belgium
        </a>
        (as majority language only in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German-speaking_Community" title="German-speaking Community">
         German-speaking Community
        </a>
        ),
       </li>
       <li>
        four
        <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_Switzerland#List" title="Cantons of Switzerland">
         cantons of Switzerland
        </a>
        , and
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">
         Luxembourg
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_the_Sprachraum">
        Outside the Sprachraum
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Outside the Sprachraum">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Although
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_and_expulsion_of_Germans" title="Flight and expulsion of Germans">
        expulsions
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Organised_persecution_of_ethnic_Germans" title="Organised persecution of ethnic Germans">
        (forced) assimilation
       </a>
       after the two
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_War" title="World War">
        World Wars
       </a>
       greatly diminished them, minority communities of mostly bilingual German native speakers exist in areas both adjacent to and detached from the Sprachraum.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Within Europe and Asia, German is a recognized minority language in the following countries:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">
         Bosnia and Herzegovina
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charter-ratifications_44-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-charter-ratifications-44">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (see also:
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donauschwaben" title="Donauschwaben">
           Donauschwaben
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">
         Czech Republic
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charter-ratifications_44-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-charter-ratifications-44">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (see also:
        <a href="/wiki/Germans_in_the_Czech_Republic" title="Germans in the Czech Republic">
         Germans in the Czech Republic
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">
         Denmark
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charter-ratifications_44-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-charter-ratifications-44">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (see also:
        <a href="/wiki/North_Schleswig_Germans" title="North Schleswig Germans">
         North Schleswig Germans
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">
         Hungary
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charter-ratifications_44-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-charter-ratifications-44">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (see also:
        <a href="/wiki/Germans_of_Hungary" title="Germans of Hungary">
         Germans of Hungary
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
         Italy
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
         <a href="#cite_note-45">
          [42]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (outside of South Tyrol; see also:
        <a href="/wiki/Cimbrian_language" title="Cimbrian language">
         Cimbrian
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B2cheno_language" title="Mòcheno language">
         Mòcheno/Fersentalerisch
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Walser_German" title="Walser German">
         Walser German
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">
         Kazakhstan
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
         <a href="#cite_note-46">
          [43]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
         <a href="#cite_note-47">
          [44]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (see also:
        <a href="/wiki/Germans_of_Kazakhstan" title="Germans of Kazakhstan">
         Germans of Kazakhstan
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">
         Poland
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charter-ratifications_44-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-charter-ratifications-44">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (see also
        <a href="/wiki/German_minority_in_Poland" title="German minority in Poland">
         German minority in Poland
        </a>
        ; German is
        <a href="/wiki/Bilingual_communes_in_Poland" title="Bilingual communes in Poland">
         auxiliary language in 31 communes
        </a>
        ;
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
         <a href="#cite_note-48">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">
         Romania
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charter-ratifications_44-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-charter-ratifications-44">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (see also:
        <a href="/wiki/Germans_of_Romania" title="Germans of Romania">
         Germans of Romania
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
         Russia
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
         <a href="#cite_note-49">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (see also:
        <a href="/wiki/History_of_Germans_in_Russia,_Ukraine_and_the_Soviet_Union" title="History of Germans in Russia, Ukraine and the Soviet Union">
         Germans in Russia
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">
         Slovakia
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charter-ratifications_44-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-charter-ratifications-44">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (see also:
        <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Germans" title="Carpathian Germans">
         Carpathian Germans
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">
         Ukraine
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charter-ratifications_44-7">
         <a href="#cite_note-charter-ratifications-44">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (see also:
        <a href="/wiki/History_of_Germans_in_Russia,_Ukraine_and_the_Soviet_Union" title="History of Germans in Russia, Ukraine and the Soviet Union">
         Germans in Ukraine
        </a>
        )
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        France
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_German" title="High German">
        High German
       </a>
       varieties of
       <a href="/wiki/Alsatian_dialect" title="Alsatian dialect">
        Alsatian
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moselle_Franconian" title="Moselle Franconian">
        Moselle Franconian
       </a>
       are identified as "
       <a href="/wiki/Regional_language" title="Regional language">
        regional languages
       </a>
       ", but the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_and_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional and Minority Languages">
        European Charter for Regional and Minority Languages
       </a>
       of 1998 has not yet been ratified by the government.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">
        Netherlands
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Limburgish_language" title="Limburgish language">
        Limburgish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Frisian_languages" title="Frisian languages">
        Frisian
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low German
       </a>
       languages are protected regional languages according to the European Charter for Regional and Minority Languages;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charter-ratifications_44-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-charter-ratifications-44">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       however, they are widely considered separate languages and neither German nor Dutch dialects.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">
        Africa
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Africa">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Namibia">
        Namibia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Namibia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NamibiaDeutscheSprache.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="557" data-file-width="841" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/NamibiaDeutscheSprache.jpg/220px-NamibiaDeutscheSprache.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/NamibiaDeutscheSprache.jpg/330px-NamibiaDeutscheSprache.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/NamibiaDeutscheSprache.jpg/440px-NamibiaDeutscheSprache.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NamibiaDeutscheSprache.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Examples of German language in
         <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">
          Namibian
         </a>
         everyday life
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/German_language_in_Namibia" title="German language in Namibia">
        German language in Namibia
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Namibia was
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_South-West_Africa" title="German South-West Africa">
        a colony
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">
        German Empire
       </a>
       from 1884 to 1919. Mostly descending from German settlers who immigrated during this time, 25–30,000 people still speak German as a native tongue today.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsch_in_Namibia_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Deutsch_in_Namibia-51">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The period of German colonialism in Namibia also led to the evolution of a Standard German-based
       <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">
        pidgin
       </a>
       language called "
       <a href="/wiki/Namibian_Black_German" title="Namibian Black German">
        Namibian Black German
       </a>
       ", which became a second language for parts of the indigenous population. Although it is nearly extinct today, some older Namibians still have some knowledge of it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       German, along with English and
       <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">
        Afrikaans
       </a>
       was a co-official language of Namibia from 1984 until its independence from
       <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">
        South Africa
       </a>
       in 1990. At this point, the Namibian government perceived Afrikaans and German as symbols of
       <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">
        apartheid
       </a>
       and colonialism, and decided English would be the sole official language, claiming that it was a "neutral" language as there were virtually no English native speakers in Namibia at that time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsch_in_Namibia_51-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Deutsch_in_Namibia-51">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       German, Afrikaans and several indigenous languages became "national languages" by law, identifying them as elements of the cultural heritage of the nation and ensuring that the state acknowledged and supported their presence in the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, German is used in a wide variety of spheres, especially business and tourism, as well as the churches (most notably the German-speaking
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_in_Namibia_(GELK)" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church in Namibia (GELK)">
        Evangelical Lutheran Church in Namibia (GELK)
       </a>
       ), schools (e.g. the
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_H%C3%B6here_Privatschule_Windhoek" title="Deutsche Höhere Privatschule Windhoek">
          Deutsche Höhere Privatschule Windhoek
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ), literature (German-Namibian authors include Giselher W. Hoffmann), radio (the
       <a href="/wiki/Namibian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Namibian Broadcasting Corporation">
        Namibian Broadcasting Corporation
       </a>
       produces radio programs in German), and music (e.g. artist
       <a href="/wiki/EES_(rapper)" title="EES (rapper)">
        EES
       </a>
       ). The
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a href="/wiki/Allgemeine_Zeitung_(Namibia)" title="Allgemeine Zeitung (Namibia)">
          Allgemeine Zeitung
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       is one of the three biggest newspapers in Namibia and the only German-language daily in Africa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsch_in_Namibia_51-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Deutsch_in_Namibia-51">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa">
        South Africa
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: South Africa">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Mostly originating from different waves of immigration during the 19th and 20th centuries, an estimated 12,000 people speak German or a German variety as a first language in
       <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">
        South Africa
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-53">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One of the largest communities consists of the speakers of "
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nataler_Deutsch&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nataler Deutsch (page does not exist)">
          Nataler Deutsch
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a variety of
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low German
       </a>
       , concentrated in and around
       <a href="/wiki/Wartburg,_KwaZulu-Natal" title="Wartburg, KwaZulu-Natal">
        Wartburg
       </a>
       . The small town of
       <a href="/wiki/Kroondal" title="Kroondal">
        Kroondal
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/North_West_(South_African_province)" title="North West (South African province)">
        North-West
       </a>
       Province also has a mostly German-speaking population. The South African constitution identifies German as a "commonly used" language and the
       <a href="/wiki/Pan_South_African_Language_Board" title="Pan South African Language Board">
        Pan South African Language Board
       </a>
       is obligated to promote and ensure respect for it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The community is strong enough that several German International schools are supported such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Schule_Pretoria" title="Deutsche Schule Pretoria">
        Deutsche Schule Pretoria
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">
        North America
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: North America">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/German_language_in_the_United_States" title="German language in the United States">
        German language in the United States
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_German_language" title="Pennsylvania German language">
        Pennsylvania German language
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plautdietsch" title="Plautdietsch">
        Plautdietsch
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Hutterite_German" title="Hutterite German">
        Hutterite German
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the United States, the states of
       <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">
        North Dakota
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">
        South Dakota
       </a>
       are the only states where German is the most common language spoken at home after English.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US_Census_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-US_Census-56">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       German geographical names can be found throughout the Midwest region of the country, such as
       <a href="/wiki/New_Ulm,_Minnesota" title="New Ulm, Minnesota">
        New Ulm
       </a>
       and many other towns in
       <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">
        Minnesota
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Bismarck,_North_Dakota" title="Bismarck, North Dakota">
        Bismarck
       </a>
       (North Dakota's state capital),
       <a href="/wiki/Munich,_North_Dakota" title="Munich, North Dakota">
        Munich
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Karlsruhe,_North_Dakota" title="Karlsruhe, North Dakota">
        Karlsruhe
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Strasburg,_North_Dakota" title="Strasburg, North Dakota">
        Strasburg
       </a>
       (named after a town near Odessa in Ukraine)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in North Dakota;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Braunfels" title="New Braunfels">
        New Braunfels
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Texas" title="Fredericksburg, Texas">
        Fredericksburg
       </a>
       , Weimar, and
       <a href="/wiki/Muenster,_Texas" title="Muenster, Texas">
        Muenster
       </a>
       in Texas; Corn (formerly Korn), Kiefer and Berlin in Oklahoma; and
       <a href="/wiki/Kiel,_Wisconsin" title="Kiel, Wisconsin">
        Kiel
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Berlin,_Wisconsin" title="Berlin, Wisconsin">
        Berlin
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Germantown,_Wisconsin" title="Germantown, Wisconsin">
        Germantown
       </a>
       in Wisconsin.
      </p>
      <p>
       Between 1843 and 1910, more than 5 million Germans emigrated overseas,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-58">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       mostly to the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       German remained an important language in churches, schools, newspapers, and even the administration of the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Brewers%27_Association" title="United States Brewers' Association">
        United States Brewers' Association
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       through the early 20th century, but was severely repressed during
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
        World War I
       </a>
       . Over the course of the 20th century, many of the descendants of 18th century and 19th century immigrants ceased speaking German at home, but small populations of speakers are still found in
       <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">
        Pennsylvania
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Amish" title="Amish">
        Amish
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hutterites" title="Hutterites">
        Hutterites
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dunkard_Township,_Greene_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Dunkard Township, Greene County, Pennsylvania">
        Dunkards
       </a>
       and some
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mennonites" title="Mennonites">
        Mennonites
       </a>
       historically spoke
       <a href="/wiki/Hutterite_German" title="Hutterite German">
        Hutterite German
       </a>
       and a
       <a href="/wiki/West_Central_German" title="West Central German">
        West Central German
       </a>
       variety of German known as
       <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_German_language" title="Pennsylvania German language">
        Pennsylvania German
       </a>
       or Pennsylvania Dutch),
       <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">
        Kansas
       </a>
       (Mennonites and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volga_German" title="Volga German">
        Volga Germans
       </a>
       ), North Dakota (Hutterite Germans, Mennonites,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Germans_in_Russia_and_the_Soviet_Union" title="History of Germans in Russia and the Soviet Union">
        Russian Germans
       </a>
       , Volga Germans, and
       <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Germans" title="Baltic Germans">
        Baltic Germans
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">
        South Dakota
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">
        Montana
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">
        Texas
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Texas_German" title="Texas German">
        Texas German
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">
        Wisconsin
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">
        Indiana
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">
        Oregon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">
        Oklahoma
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">
        Ohio
       </a>
       (72,570).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="Given citation does not provide any useful information (January 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       A significant group of German
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pietists" title="Pietists">
        Pietists
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">
        Iowa
       </a>
       formed the
       <a href="/wiki/Amana_Colonies" title="Amana Colonies">
        Amana Colonies
       </a>
       and continue to practice speaking their heritage language. Early twentieth century immigration was often to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri">
        St. Louis
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">
        Chicago
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">
        New York
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee" title="Milwaukee">
        Milwaukee
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">
        Pittsburgh
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">
        Cincinnati
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-005795,_Deutsche_Zeitungen_in_Nordamerika.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="712" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-005795%2C_Deutsche_Zeitungen_in_Nordamerika.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-005795%2C_Deutsche_Zeitungen_in_Nordamerika.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-005795%2C_Deutsche_Zeitungen_in_Nordamerika.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-005795%2C_Deutsche_Zeitungen_in_Nordamerika.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-005795%2C_Deutsche_Zeitungen_in_Nordamerika.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-005795%2C_Deutsche_Zeitungen_in_Nordamerika.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-005795,_Deutsche_Zeitungen_in_Nordamerika.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_in_the_United_States#German-language_press" title="German in the United States">
          German-language newspapers in the U.S.
         </a>
         in 1922
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The dialects of German which are or were primarily spoken in colonies or communities founded by German-speaking people resemble the dialects of the regions the founders came from. For example, Hutterite German resembles dialects of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carinthia_(state)" title="Carinthia (state)">
        Carinthia
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Texas_German" title="Texas German">
        Texas German
       </a>
       is a dialect spoken in the areas of Texas settled by the
       <a href="/wiki/Adelsverein" title="Adelsverein">
        Adelsverein
       </a>
       , such as New Braunfels and Fredericksburg. In the
       <a href="/wiki/Amana_Colonies" title="Amana Colonies">
        Amana Colonies
       </a>
       in the state of Iowa,
       <a href="/wiki/Amana_German" title="Amana German">
        Amana German
       </a>
       is spoken.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plautdietsch" title="Plautdietsch">
        Plautdietsch
       </a>
       is a large
       <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">
        minority language
       </a>
       spoken in Northern Mexico by the
       <a href="/wiki/Mennonite" title="Mennonite">
        Mennonite
       </a>
       communities, and is spoken by more than 200,000 people in Mexico.
       <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_German_language" title="Pennsylvania German language">
        Pennsylvania German
       </a>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/West_Central_German" title="West Central German">
        West Central German
       </a>
       dialect spoken by most of the
       <a href="/wiki/Amish" title="Amish">
        Amish
       </a>
       population of Pennsylvania, Ohio, and Indiana and resembles
       <a href="/wiki/Palatine_German_language" title="Palatine German language">
        Palatinate German
       </a>
       dialects.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Hutterite_German" title="Hutterite German">
        Hutterite German
       </a>
       is an Upper German dialect of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austro-Bavarian" title="Austro-Bavarian">
        Austro-Bavarian
       </a>
       variety of the German language, which is spoken by Hutterite communities in Canada and the United States. Hutterite is spoken in the U.S. states of
       <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">
        Washington
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">
        Montana
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">
        North Dakota
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">
        South Dakota
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">
        Minnesota
       </a>
       ; and in the Canadian provinces of
       <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">
        Alberta
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">
        Saskatchewan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">
        Manitoba
       </a>
       . Its speakers belong to some Schmiedleit, Lehrerleit, and Dariusleit Hutterite groups, but there are also speakers among the older generations of Prairieleit (the descendants of those Hutterites who chose not to settle in colonies). Hutterite children who grow up in the colonies learn to speak Hutterite German before learning English, the standard language of the surrounding areas, in school. Many of these children, though, continue with German Grammar School, in addition to public school, throughout a student's elementary education.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
        Canada
       </a>
       , there are 622,650 speakers of German according to the most recent census in 2006,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statcan_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Statcan-62">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with people of German ancestry (
       <a href="/wiki/German_Canadians" title="German Canadians">
        German Canadians
       </a>
       ) found throughout the country. German-speaking communities are particularly found in
       <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">
        British Columbia
       </a>
       (118,035) and
       <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">
        Ontario
       </a>
       (230,330).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statcan_62-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Statcan-62">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is a large and vibrant community in the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Kitchener,_Ontario" title="Kitchener, Ontario">
        Kitchener, Ontario
       </a>
       , which was at one point named Berlin. German immigrants were instrumental in the country's three largest urban areas:
       <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">
        Montreal
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">
        Toronto
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">
        Vancouver
       </a>
       ; post-
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">
        Second World War
       </a>
       immigrants managed to preserve a fluency in the German language in their respective neighborhoods and sections. In the first half of the 20th century, over a million
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German-Canadian" title="German-Canadian">
        German-Canadians
       </a>
       made the language Canada's third most spoken after
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In Mexico there are also large populations of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Mexican" title="German Mexican">
        German ancestry
       </a>
       , mainly in the cities of:
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">
        Mexico City
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Puebla" title="Puebla">
        Puebla
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mazatl%C3%A1n" title="Mazatlán">
        Mazatlán
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tapachula" title="Tapachula">
        Tapachula
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ecatepec_de_Morelos" title="Ecatepec de Morelos">
        Ecatepec de Morelos
       </a>
       , and larger populations scattered in the states of
       <a href="/wiki/Chihuahua_(state)" title="Chihuahua (state)">
        Chihuahua
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Durango" title="Durango">
        Durango
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Zacatecas" title="Zacatecas">
        Zacatecas
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">
        South America
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: South America">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pomeranoespiritosanto.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="986" data-file-width="677" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Pomeranoespiritosanto.svg/220px-Pomeranoespiritosanto.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Pomeranoespiritosanto.svg/330px-Pomeranoespiritosanto.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Pomeranoespiritosanto.svg/440px-Pomeranoespiritosanto.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pomeranoespiritosanto.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Municipality" title="Municipality">
          Municipalities
         </a>
         where
         <a href="/wiki/East_Pomeranian_dialect" title="East Pomeranian dialect">
          East Pomeranian
         </a>
         is co-official in
         <a href="/wiki/Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Espírito Santo">
          Espírito Santo
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
          Brazil
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_German" title="Brazilian German">
        Brazilian German
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Colonia_Tovar_dialect" title="Colonia Tovar dialect">
        Colonia Tovar dialect
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In Brazil, the largest concentrations of German speakers are in the states of
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">
        Rio Grande do Sul
       </a>
       (where
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riograndenser_Hunsr%C3%BCckisch" title="Riograndenser Hunsrückisch">
        Riograndenser Hunsrückisch
       </a>
       developed),
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Catarina_(state)" title="Santa Catarina (state)">
        Santa Catarina
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_(state)" title="Paraná (state)">
        Paraná
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">
        São Paulo
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Espírito Santo">
        Espírito Santo
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipol_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ipol-66">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are also important concentrations of German-speaking descendants in
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">
        Chile
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">
        Paraguay
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">
        Venezuela
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">
        Peru
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">
        Bolivia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_53-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-53">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 20th century, over 100,000 German
       <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">
        political refugees
       </a>
       and invited entrepreneurs settled in
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">
        Latin America
       </a>
       , in countries such as
       <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">
        Costa Rica
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">
        Panama
       </a>
       , Venezuela, and the Dominican Republic, to establish German-speaking enclaves, and reportedly there is a small
       <a href="/wiki/German_immigration_to_Puerto_Rico" title="German immigration to Puerto Rico">
        German immigration to Puerto Rico
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       In most locations where German immigrants settled, the vast majority of their descendants no longer speak German, as they have been largely assimilated into the host language and culture of the specific location of settlement; generally Spanish or Portuguese.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Co-official_statuses_of_German_or_German_varieties_in_Brazil">
        Co-official statuses of German or German varieties in Brazil
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Co-official statuses of German or German varieties in Brazil">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Espírito Santo">
         Espírito Santo
        </a>
        (statewide cultural language)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipol_66-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-ipol-66">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ES-legislation_67-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-ES-legislation-67">
          [64]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
         <a href="#cite_note-68">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
         <a href="#cite_note-69">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Domingos_Martins" title="Domingos Martins">
           Domingos Martins
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Laranja_da_Terra" title="Laranja da Terra">

          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pancas,_Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Pancas, Espírito Santo">
           Pancas
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_de_Jetib%C3%A1" title="Santa Maria de Jetibá">
           Santa Maria de Jetibá
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Vila_Pav%C3%A3o" title="Vila Pavão">
           Vila Pavão
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">
         Rio Grande do Sul
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipol_66-2">

          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <ul>

          </a>
         </li>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">
        Oceania
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Oceania">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
        Australia
       </a>
       , the state of
       <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">
        South Australia
       </a>
       experienced a pronounced wave of immigration in the 1840s from Prussia (particularly the
       <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">
        Silesia
       </a>
       region). With the prolonged isolation from other German speakers and contact with
       <a href="/wiki/Australian_English" title="Australian English">
        Australian English
       </a>
       , a unique dialect known as
       <a href="/wiki/Barossa_German" title="Barossa German">
        Barossa German
       </a>
       has developed and is spoken predominantly in the
       <a href="/wiki/Barossa_Valley" title="Barossa Valley">
        Barossa Valley
       </a>
       near
       <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">
        Adelaide
       </a>
       . Usage of German sharply declined with the advent of
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
        World War I
       </a>
       , due to the prevailing anti-German sentiment in the population and related government action. It continued to be used as a first language into the twentieth century but now its use is limited to a few older speakers.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       German migration to New Zealand in the 19th century was less pronounced than migration from Britain, Ireland, and perhaps even Scandinavia. Despite this there were significant pockets of German-speaking communities which lasted until the first decades of the 20th century. German-speakers settled principally in
       <a href="/wiki/Puhoi" title="Puhoi">
        Puhoi
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nelson,_New_Zealand" title="Nelson, New Zealand">
        Nelson
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Gore,_New_Zealand" title="Gore, New Zealand">
        Gore
       </a>
       . At the last census (2006), 37,500 people in New Zealand spoke German, making it the third most spoken European language after English and French and overall the ninth most spoken language.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is also an important German
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German-based_creole_languages" title="German-based creole languages">
        creole
       </a>
       being studied and recovered, named
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unserdeutsch" title="Unserdeutsch">
          Unserdeutsch
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       , spoken in the former German colony of
       <a href="/wiki/German_New_Guinea" title="German New Guinea">
        German New Guinea
       </a>
       , across
       <a href="/wiki/Micronesia" title="Micronesia">
        Micronesia
       </a>
       and in northern Australia (i.e. coastal parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">
        Queensland
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">
        Western Australia
       </a>
       ), by a few elderly people. The risk of its extinction is serious and efforts to revive interest in the language are being implemented by scholars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holm,_1989_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holm,_1989-70">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="German_as_a_foreign_language">
        German as a foreign language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: German as a foreign language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:German_foreign_language_EU.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="377" data-file-width="803" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/German_foreign_language_EU.jpg/220px-German_foreign_language_EU.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/German_foreign_language_EU.jpg/330px-German_foreign_language_EU.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/German_foreign_language_EU.jpg/440px-German_foreign_language_EU.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:German_foreign_language_EU.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Knowledge of German as a
         <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language" title="Foreign language">
          foreign language
         </a>
         in the
         <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
          EU
         </a>
         member states (+
         <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
          Turkey
         </a>
         ), in per cent of the adult population (+15), 2005.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Like
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       , German has become a classic second foreign language in the western world, as
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       in the US) is well established as the first foreign language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurobarometer_3-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-eurobarometer-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goethe-institut_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-goethe-institut-71">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       German ranks second (after English) among the best known foreign languages in the EU (on a par with French)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurobarometer_3-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-eurobarometer-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as well as in
       <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
        Russia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levada2008_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Levada2008-72">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In terms of student numbers across all levels of education, German ranks third in the EU (after English and French)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurostat_39-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-eurostat-39">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as well as in the United States (after Spanish and French).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLA_12-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-MLA-12">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2015, approximately 15.4 million people were in the process of learning German across all levels of education worldwide.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goethe-institut_71-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-goethe-institut-71">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As this number remained relatively stable since 2005 (± 1 million), roughly 75–100 million people able to communicate in German as foreign language can be inferred assuming an average course duration of three years and other estimated parameters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to a 2012 survey, 47 million people within the EU (i.e., up to two thirds of the 75-100 million worldwide) claimed to have sufficient German skills to have a conversation. Within the EU, not counting countries where it is an official language, German as a foreign language is most popular in
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">
        Eastern
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">
        Northern Europe
       </a>
       , namely the
       <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">
        Czech Republic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">
        Croatia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">
        Denmark
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">
        Netherlands
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">
        Slovakia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">
        Hungary
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">
        Slovenia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">
        Sweden
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">
        Poland
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurobarometer_3-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-eurobarometer-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurostat_39-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-eurostat-39">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       German was once and, to some extent, is still, a
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       in those parts of Europe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polenz1999_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Polenz1999-74">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_German">
        Standard German
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Standard German">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">
        Standard German
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Standard German originated not as a traditional dialect of a specific region but as a
       <a href="/wiki/Written_language" title="Written language">
        written language
       </a>
       . However, there are places where the traditional regional dialects have been replaced by new vernaculars based on standard German; that is the case in large stretches of
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Germany" title="Northern Germany">
        Northern Germany
       </a>
       but also in major cities in other parts of the country. It is important to note, however, that the colloquial standard German differs greatly from the formal written language, especially in grammar and syntax, in which it has been influenced by dialectal speech.
      </p>
      <p>
       Standard German differs regionally between German-speaking countries in
       <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">
        vocabulary
       </a>
       and some instances of
       <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation" title="Pronunciation">
        pronunciation
       </a>
       and even
       <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">
        grammar
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">
        orthography
       </a>
       . This variation must not be confused with the variation of local
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dialects" title="Dialects">
        dialects
       </a>
       . Even though the regional varieties of standard German are only somewhat influenced by the local dialects, they are very distinct. German is thus considered a
       <a href="/wiki/Pluricentric_language" title="Pluricentric language">
        pluricentric language
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In most regions, the speakers use a continuum from more dialectal varieties to more standard varieties according to circumstances.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties_of_Standard_German">
        Varieties of Standard German
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Varieties of Standard German">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:German_standard_varieties.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="1030" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/German_standard_varieties.png/220px-German_standard_varieties.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/German_standard_varieties.png/330px-German_standard_varieties.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/German_standard_varieties.png/440px-German_standard_varieties.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:German_standard_varieties.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The national and regional standard varieties of German.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
          <a href="#cite_note-75">
           [72]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In German
       <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">
        linguistics
       </a>
       , German
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">
        dialects
       </a>
       are distinguished from
       <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">
        varieties
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">
        standard German
       </a>
       . The
       <i>
        varieties of standard German
       </i>
       refer to the different local varieties of the
       <a href="/wiki/Pluricentric_language" title="Pluricentric language">
        pluricentric
       </a>
       standard German. They differ only slightly in lexicon and phonology. In certain regions, they have replaced the traditional German dialects, especially in Northern Germany.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/German_Standard_German" title="German Standard German">
         German Standard German
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austrian_Standard_German" title="Austrian Standard German">
         Austrian Standard German
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Standard_German" title="Swiss Standard German">
         Swiss Standard German
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In the German-speaking parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">
        Switzerland
       </a>
       , mixtures of dialect and standard are very seldom used, and the use of Standard German is largely restricted to the written language, though about 10% of the Swiss residents speak
       <i>
        High German
       </i>
       (aka Standard German) at home, but mainly due to German immigrants.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This situation has been called a
       <i>
        medial
        <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">
         diglossia
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Standard_German" title="Swiss Standard German">
        Swiss Standard German
       </a>
       is used in the Swiss education system, whereas
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austrian_Standard_German" title="Austrian Standard German">
        Austrian Standard German
       </a>
       is officially used in the Austrian education system.
      </p>
      <p>
       A mixture of dialect and standard does not normally occur in Northern Germany either. The traditional varieties there are Low German, whereas Standard German is a High German "variety". Because their linguistic distance to it is greater, they do not mesh with Standard German the way that High German dialects (such as Bavarian, Swabian, Hessian) can.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">
        Dialects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Dialects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/German_dialects" title="German dialects">
        German dialects
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Continental_West_Germanic_languages.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1166" data-file-width="898" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Continental_West_Germanic_languages.png/180px-Continental_West_Germanic_languages.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Continental_West_Germanic_languages.png/270px-Continental_West_Germanic_languages.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Continental_West_Germanic_languages.png/360px-Continental_West_Germanic_languages.png 2x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Continental_West_Germanic_languages.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The continental
         <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
          West Germanic
         </a>
         dialects
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       German is a member of the
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
        West Germanic language
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">
        family of languages
       </a>
       , which in turn is part of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_language_family" title="Indo-European language family">
        Indo-European language family
       </a>
       . The German dialects are the traditional local varieties; many of them are hardly understandable to someone who knows only standard German, and they have great differences in
       <a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">
        lexicon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">
        phonology
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">
        syntax
       </a>
       . If a narrow definition of
       <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">
        language
       </a>
       based on
       <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">
        mutual intelligibility
       </a>
       is used, many German dialects are considered to be separate languages (for instance in the
       <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">
        Ethnologue
       </a>
       ). However, such a point of view is unusual in German linguistics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammon,_2014_2-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ammon,_2014-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The German dialect continuum is traditionally divided most broadly into
       <a href="/wiki/High_German_languages" title="High German languages">
        High German
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low German
       </a>
       , also called
       <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxony" title="Lower Saxony">
        Low Saxon
       </a>
       . However, historically, High German dialects and Low Saxon/Low German dialects do not belong to the same language. Nevertheless, in today's Germany, Low Saxon/Low German is often perceived as a dialectal variation of Standard German on a functional level even by many native speakers. The same phenomenon is found in the eastern Netherlands, as the traditional dialects are not always identified with their Low Saxon/Low German origins, but with Dutch.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The variation among the German dialects is considerable, with often only neighbouring dialects being mutually intelligible. Some dialects are not intelligible to people who know only Standard German. However, all German dialects belong to the dialect continuum of High German and Low Saxon.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Low_German">
        Low German
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Low German">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low German
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Low_German_Dialects.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="700" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Map_of_the_Low_German_Dialects.svg/180px-Map_of_the_Low_German_Dialects.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Map_of_the_Low_German_Dialects.svg/270px-Map_of_the_Low_German_Dialects.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Map_of_the_Low_German_Dialects.svg/360px-Map_of_the_Low_German_Dialects.svg.png 2x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Low_German_Dialects.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
          Low German
         </a>
         /Low Saxon (yellow) and
         <a href="/wiki/Low_Franconian_languages" title="Low Franconian languages">
          Low Franconian
         </a>
         (orange) dialects
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Low_German" title="Middle Low German">
        Middle Low German
       </a>
       was the
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">
        Hanseatic League
       </a>
       . It was the predominant language in Northern Germany until the 16th century. In 1534, the
       <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">
        Luther Bible
       </a>
       was published. The translation is considered to be an important step towards the evolution of the
       <a href="/wiki/Early_New_High_German" title="Early New High German">
        Early New High German
       </a>
       . It aimed to be understandable to a broad audience and was based mainly on
       <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">
        Central
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">
        Upper German
       </a>
       varieties. The Early New High German language gained more prestige than
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low German
       </a>
       and became the language of science and literature. Around the same time, the Hanseatic League, based around northern ports, lost its importance as new trade routes to
       <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">
        Asia
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">
        Americas
       </a>
       were established, and the most powerful German states of that period were located in Middle and Southern Germany.
      </p>
      <p>
       The 18th and 19th centuries were marked by mass
       <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">
        education
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">
        Standard German
       </a>
       in schools. Gradually, Low German came to be politically viewed as a mere dialect spoken by the uneducated. Today, Low Saxon can be divided in two groups: Low Saxon varieties with a reasonable Standard German influx
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2012)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       and varieties of Standard German with a Low Saxon influence known as
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a href="/wiki/Missingsch" title="Missingsch">
          Missingsch
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       . Sometimes, Low Saxon and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_Franconian" title="Low Franconian">
        Low Franconian
       </a>
       varieties are grouped together because both are unaffected by the High German consonant shift. However, the proportion of the population who can understand and speak it has decreased continuously since
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       . The largest cities in the Low German area are
       <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">
        Hamburg
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Dortmund" title="Dortmund">
        Dortmund
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Low_Franconian">
        Low Franconian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Low Franconian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="2560" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png/180px-Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png/270px-Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png/360px-Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png 2x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Low_Franconian_languages" title="Low Franconian languages">
          Low Franconian dialects
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Low_Franconian_languages" title="Low Franconian languages">
        Low Franconian
       </a>
       dialects are the dialects that are more closely related to
