       <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">
        Dutch
       </a>
       than to
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low German
       </a>
       . Most of the Low Franconian dialects are spoken in the
       <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">
        Netherlands
       </a>
       and in
       <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">
        Belgium
       </a>
       , where they are considered as dialects of Dutch, which is itself a Low Franconian language. In Germany, Low Franconian dialects are spoken in the northwest of
       <a href="/wiki/North_Rhine-Westphalia" title="North Rhine-Westphalia">
        North Rhine-Westphalia
       </a>
       , along the
       <a href="/wiki/Lower_Rhine" title="Lower Rhine">
        Lower Rhine
       </a>
       . The Low Franconian dialects spoken in Germany are referred to as
       <a href="/wiki/Meuse-Rhenish" title="Meuse-Rhenish">
        Meuse-Rhenish
       </a>
       or Low Rhenish. In the north of the German Low Franconian language area, North Low Franconian dialects (also referred to as Cleverlands or as dialects of
       <a href="/wiki/South_Guelderish" title="South Guelderish">
        South Guelderish
       </a>
       ) are spoken. These dialects are more closely related to Dutch (also North Low Franconian) than the South Low Franconian dialects (also referred to as East
       <a href="/wiki/Limburgish_language" title="Limburgish language">
        Limburgish
       </a>
       and, east of the
       <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">
        Rhine
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Low_Bergish" title="Low Bergish">
        Low Bergish
       </a>
       ), which are spoken in the south of the German Low Franconian language area. The South Low Franconian dialects are more closely related to Limburgish than to Dutch, and are transitional dialects between Low Franconian and
       <a href="/wiki/Ripuarian_language" title="Ripuarian language">
        Ripuarian
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Franconian_dialects" title="Central Franconian dialects">
        Central Franconian
       </a>
       ). The
       <a href="/wiki/East_Bergish" title="East Bergish">
        East Bergish
       </a>
       dialects are the easternmost Low Franconian dialects, and are transitional dialects between North- and South Low Franconian, and
       <a href="/wiki/Westphalian_language" title="Westphalian language">
        Westphalian
       </a>
       (Low German), with most of its features however being North Low Franconian. The largest cities in the German Low Franconian area are
       <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">
        Düsseldorf
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Duisburg" title="Duisburg">
        Duisburg
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="High_German">
        High German
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: High German">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/High_German_languages" title="High German languages">
        High German languages
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1053" data-file-width="745" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png/180px-Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png/270px-Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png/360px-Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png 2x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">
          Central German
         </a>
         dialects
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="652" data-file-width="652" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png/180px-Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png/270px-Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png/360px-Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png 2x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Franconian_languages" title="Franconian languages">
          Franconian dialects
         </a>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Low_Franconian_languages" title="Low Franconian languages">
          Low Franconian
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Central_Franconian_dialects" title="Central Franconian dialects">
          Central-
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Rhine_Franconian_dialects" title="Rhine Franconian dialects">
          Rhine Franconian
         </a>
         , and
         <a href="/wiki/High_Franconian_German" title="High Franconian German">
          High Franconian)
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The High German dialects consist of the
       <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">
        Central German
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/High_Franconian_German" title="High Franconian German">
        High Franconian
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_German_language" title="Upper German language">
        Upper German
       </a>
       dialects. The High Franconian dialects are transitional dialects between Central- and Upper German. The High German varieties spoken by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jew" title="Ashkenazi Jew">
        Ashkenazi Jews
       </a>
       have several unique features, and are considered as a separate language,
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       , written with the
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">
        Hebrew alphabet
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Central_German">
        Central German
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Central German">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">
        Central German
       </a>
       dialects are spoken in Central Germany, from
       <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">
        Aachen
       </a>
       in the west to
       <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6rlitz" title="Görlitz">
        Görlitz
       </a>
       in the east. They consist of
       <a href="/wiki/Franconian_languages" title="Franconian languages">
        Franconian dialects
       </a>
       in the west (
       <a href="/wiki/West_Central_German" title="West Central German">
        West Central German
       </a>
       ), and non-Franconian dialects in the east (
       <a href="/wiki/East_Central_German" title="East Central German">
        East Central German
       </a>
       ). Modern Standard German is mostly based on Central German dialects.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Franconian,
       <b>
        West Central German
       </b>
       dialects are the
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Franconian_dialects" title="Central Franconian dialects">
        Central Franconian dialects
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Ripuarian_language" title="Ripuarian language">
        Ripuarian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Moselle_Franconian_dialects" title="Moselle Franconian dialects">
        Moselle Franconian
       </a>
       ), and the
       <a href="/wiki/Rhine_Franconian_dialects" title="Rhine Franconian dialects">
        Rhine Franconian dialects
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Hessian_dialects" title="Hessian dialects">
        Hessian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Palatine_German_language" title="Palatine German language">
        Palatine
       </a>
       ). These dialects are considered as
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        German in Germany and Belgium
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luxembourgish_language" title="Luxembourgish language">
         Luxembourgish
        </a>
        in Luxembourg
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Lorraine_Franconian" title="Lorraine Franconian">
         Lorraine Franconian
        </a>
        (spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Moselle_(department)" title="Moselle (department)">
         Moselle
        </a>
        ) and as a Rhine Franconian variant of
        <a href="/wiki/Alsatian_dialect" title="Alsatian dialect">
         Alsatian
        </a>
        (spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Alsace_bossue" title="Alsace bossue">
         Alsace bossue
        </a>
        only) in France
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Limburgish_language" title="Limburgish language">
         Limburgish
        </a>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Kerkrade_dialect" title="Kerkrade dialect">
         Kerkrade dialect
        </a>
        in the Netherlands.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Luxembourgish as well as the
       <a href="/wiki/Transylvanian_Saxon_dialect" title="Transylvanian Saxon dialect">
        Transylvanian Saxon dialect
       </a>
       spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">
        Transylvania
       </a>
       are based on Moselle Franconian dialects. The largest cities in the Franconian Central German area are
       <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">
        Cologne
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">
        Frankfurt
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Further east, the non-Franconian,
       <b>
        East Central German
       </b>
       dialects are spoken (
       <a href="/wiki/Thuringian_dialect" title="Thuringian dialect">
        Thuringian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Saxon_German" title="Upper Saxon German">
        Upper Saxon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Erzgebirgisch" title="Erzgebirgisch">
        Ore Mountainian
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Lausitzisch-neum%C3%A4rkisch_dialects" title="Lausitzisch-neumärkisch dialects">
        Lusatian-New Markish
       </a>
       , and earlier, in the then German-speaking parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">
        Silesia
       </a>
       also
       <a href="/wiki/Silesian_German" title="Silesian German">
        Silesian
       </a>
       , and in then German southern
       <a href="/wiki/East_Prussia" title="East Prussia">
        East Prussia
       </a>
       also
       <a href="/wiki/High_Prussian_dialect" title="High Prussian dialect">
        High Prussian
       </a>
       ). The largest cities in the East Central German area are
       <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">
        Berlin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">
        Leipzig
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="High_Franconian">
        High Franconian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: High Franconian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/High_Franconian_German" title="High Franconian German">
        High Franconian dialects
       </a>
       are transitional dialects between Central- and Upper German. They consist of the
       <a href="/wiki/East_Franconian_German" title="East Franconian German">
        East-
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/South_Franconian_German" title="South Franconian German">
        South Franconian
       </a>
       dialects.
      </p>
      <p>
       The East Franconian dialect branch is one of the most spoken dialect branches in Germany. These dialects are spoken in the region of
       <a href="/wiki/Franconia" title="Franconia">
        Franconia
       </a>
       and in the central parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">
        Saxon
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Vogtland" title="Vogtland">
        Vogtland
       </a>
       . Franconia consists of the
       <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">
        Bavarian
       </a>
       districts of
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Franconia" title="Upper Franconia">
        Upper-
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Franconia" title="Middle Franconia">
        Middle-
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Lower_Franconia" title="Lower Franconia">
        Lower Franconia
       </a>
       , the region of
       <a href="/wiki/South_Thuringia" title="South Thuringia">
        South Thuringia
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">
        Thuringia
       </a>
       ), and the eastern parts of the region of
       <a href="/wiki/Heilbronn-Franken" title="Heilbronn-Franken">
        Heilbronn-Franken
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Tauber_Franconia" title="Tauber Franconia">
        Tauber Franconia
       </a>
       and Hohenlohe) in
       <a href="/wiki/Baden-W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Baden-Württemberg">
        Baden-Württemberg
       </a>
       . The largest cities in the East Franconian area are
       <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">
        Nuremberg
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrzburg" title="Würzburg">
        Würzburg
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       South Franconian is mainly spoken in northern Baden-Württemberg in Germany, but also in the northeasternmost part of the region of
       <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">
        Alsace
       </a>
       in France. While these dialects are considered as dialects of German in Baden-Württemberg, they are considered as dialects of
       <a href="/wiki/Alsatian_dialect" title="Alsatian dialect">
        Alsatian
       </a>
       in Alsace (most Alsatian dialects are however
       <a href="/wiki/Low_Alemannic_German" title="Low Alemannic German">
        Low Alemannic
       </a>
       ). The largest cities in the South Franconian area are
       <a href="/wiki/Karlsruhe" title="Karlsruhe">
        Karlsruhe
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Heilbronn" title="Heilbronn">
        Heilbronn
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Upper_German">
        Upper German
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Upper German">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oberdeutsche_Dialekte.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1053" data-file-width="745" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Oberdeutsche_Dialekte.png/180px-Oberdeutsche_Dialekte.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Oberdeutsche_Dialekte.png/270px-Oberdeutsche_Dialekte.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Oberdeutsche_Dialekte.png/360px-Oberdeutsche_Dialekte.png 2x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oberdeutsche_Dialekte.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">
          Upper German
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/High_Franconian_German" title="High Franconian German">
          High Franconian
         </a>
         (transitional between Central- and Upper German) dialects
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">
        Upper German
       </a>
       dialects are the
       <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">
        Alemannic dialects
       </a>
       in the west, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Bavarian_language" title="Bavarian language">
        Bavarian dialects
       </a>
       in the east.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Alemannic">
        Alemannic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Alemannic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Alemannic dialects are spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">
        Switzerland
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/High_Alemannic_German" title="High Alemannic German">
        High Alemannic
       </a>
       in the densely populated
       <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Plateau" title="Swiss Plateau">
        Swiss Plateau
       </a>
       , in the south also
       <a href="/wiki/Highest_Alemannic_German" title="Highest Alemannic German">
        Highest Alemannic
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Low_Alemannic_German" title="Low Alemannic German">
        Low Alemannic
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">
        Basel
       </a>
       ), Baden-Württemberg (
       <a href="/wiki/Swabian_German" title="Swabian German">
        Swabian
       </a>
       and Low Alemannic, in the southwest also High Alemannic),
       <a href="/wiki/Swabia_(Bavaria)" title="Swabia (Bavaria)">
        Bavarian Swabia
       </a>
       (Swabian, in the southwesternmost part also Low Alemannic),
       <a href="/wiki/Vorarlberg" title="Vorarlberg">
        Vorarlberg
       </a>
       (Low-, High-, and Highest Alemannic),
       <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">
        Alsace
       </a>
       (Low Alemannic, in the southernmost part also High Alemannic),
       <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">
        Liechtenstein
       </a>
       (High- and Highest Alemannic), and in the
       <a href="/wiki/Tyrol_(state)" title="Tyrol (state)">
        Tyrolean
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Reutte_District" title="Reutte District">
        district of Reutte
       </a>
       (Swabian). The Alemannic dialects are considered as
       <a href="/wiki/Alsatian_dialect" title="Alsatian dialect">
        Alsatian
       </a>
       in Alsace. The largest cities in the Alemannic area are
       <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">
        Stuttgart
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">
        Zürich
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Bavarian">
        Bavarian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Bavarian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Bavarian dialects are spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
        Austria
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">
        Vienna
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Lower_Austria" title="Lower Austria">
        Lower-
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Austria" title="Upper Austria">
        Upper Austria
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Styria" title="Styria">
        Styria
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Carinthia" title="Carinthia">
        Carinthia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Salzburg_(state)" title="Salzburg (state)">
        Salzburg
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Burgenland" title="Burgenland">
        Burgenland
       </a>
       , and in most parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Tyrol_(state)" title="Tyrol (state)">
        Tyrol
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">
        Bavaria
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Bavaria" title="Upper Bavaria">
        Upper-
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lower_Bavaria" title="Lower Bavaria">
        Lower Bavaria
       </a>
       as well as
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Palatinate" title="Upper Palatinate">
        Upper Palatinate
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/South_Tyrol" title="South Tyrol">
        South Tyrol
       </a>
       , southwesternmost
       <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">
        Saxony
       </a>
       (Southern
       <a href="/wiki/Vogtl%C3%A4ndisch_dialect" title="Vogtländisch dialect">
        Vogtlandian
       </a>
       ), and in the Swiss village of
       <a href="/wiki/Samnaun" title="Samnaun">
        Samnaun
       </a>
       . The largest cities in the Bavarian area are
       <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">
        Vienna
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">
        Munich
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/German_grammar" title="German grammar">
        German grammar
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       German is a
       <a href="/wiki/Fusional_language" title="Fusional language">
        fusional language
       </a>
       with a moderate degree of
       <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">
        inflection
       </a>
       , with three
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
        grammatical genders
       </a>
       ; as such, there can be a large number of words derived from the same root.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Noun_inflection">
        Noun inflection
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Noun inflection">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DE-def-art-declensions-en.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="1152" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DE-def-art-declensions-en.svg/220px-DE-def-art-declensions-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DE-def-art-declensions-en.svg/330px-DE-def-art-declensions-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DE-def-art-declensions-en.svg/440px-DE-def-art-declensions-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DE-def-art-declensions-en.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Declension of the German definite articles (all equivalent to English "the").
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender_in_German" title="Grammatical gender in German">
        Grammatical gender in German
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/German_nouns" title="German nouns">
        German nouns
       </a>
       inflect by case, gender and number:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        four
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">
         cases
        </a>
        :
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nominative" title="Nominative">
         nominative
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">
         accusative
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genitive" title="Genitive">
         genitive
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">
         dative
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        three
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
         genders
        </a>
        : masculine, feminine and neuter. Word endings sometimes reveal grammatical gender: for instance, nouns ending in
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          -ung
         </span>
        </i>
        (-ing),
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          -schaft
         </span>
        </i>
        (-ship),
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          -keit
         </span>
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          heit
         </span>
        </i>
        (-hood, -ness) are feminine, and nouns ending in
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          -chen
         </span>
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          -lein
         </span>
        </i>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Diminutive" title="Diminutive">
         diminutive
        </a>
        forms) are neuter and nouns ending in
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          -ismus
         </span>
        </i>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/-ism" title="-ism">
         -ism
        </a>
        ) are masculine. Others are more variable, sometimes depending on the region in which the language is spoken; and some endings are not restricted to one gender, e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          -er
         </span>
        </i>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Agent_noun" title="Agent noun">
         -er
        </a>
        ), e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Feier
         </span>
        </i>
        (feminine), celebration, party,
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Arbeiter
         </span>
        </i>
        (masculine), labourer, and
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Gewitter
         </span>
        </i>
        (neuter), thunderstorm.
       </li>
       <li>
        two numbers: singular and plural.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       This degree of inflection is considerably less than in
       <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">
        Old High German
       </a>
       and other old
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European languages
       </a>
       such as
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
        Ancient Greek
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">
        Sanskrit
       </a>
       , and it is also somewhat less than, for instance,
       <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">
        Old English
       </a>
       , modern
       <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">
        Icelandic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">
        Russian
       </a>
       . The three genders have collapsed in the plural. With four cases and three genders plus plural, there are 16 permutations of case and gender/number, but there are only six forms of the
       <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">
        definite article
       </a>
       , which together cover all 16 permutations. In nouns, inflection for case is required in the singular for strong masculine and neuter nouns, in the genitive and sometimes in the dative. Both of these cases are losing ground to substitutes in
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">
        informal speech
       </a>
       . The dative noun ending is considered somewhat old-fashioned in many contexts and is often dropped, but it is still used in proverbs and the like, in formal speech and in written language. Weak masculine nouns share a common case ending for genitive, dative and accusative in the singular. Feminine nouns are not declined in the singular. The plural has an inflection for the dative. In total, seven inflectional endings (not counting plural markers) exist in German:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         -s, -es, -n, -ns, -en, -ens, -e
        </span>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In German orthography, nouns and most words with the syntactical function of nouns are capitalised to make it easier for readers to determine the function of a word within a sentence (
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Am Freitag ging ich einkaufen.
        </span>
       </i>
       – "On Friday I went shopping.";
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Eines Tages kreuzte er endlich auf.
        </span>
       </i>
       – "One day he finally showed up.") This convention is almost unique to German today (shared perhaps only by the closely related
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luxembourgish_language" title="Luxembourgish language">
        Luxembourgish language
       </a>
       and several insular dialects of the
       <a href="/wiki/North_Frisian_language" title="North Frisian language">
        North Frisian language
       </a>
       ), but it was historically common in other languages such as Danish (which abolished the capitalization of nouns in 1948) and English.
      </p>
      <p>
       Like the other Germanic languages, German forms noun
       <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">
        compounds
       </a>
       in which the first noun modifies the category given by the second,:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Hundehütte
        </span>
       </i>
       ("dog hut"; specifically: "dog kennel"). Unlike English, whose newer compounds or combinations of longer nouns are often written in "open" with separating spaces, German (like some other Germanic languages) nearly always uses the "closed" form without spaces, for example:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Baumhaus
        </span>
       </i>
       ("tree house"). Like English, German allows arbitrarily long compounds in theory (see also
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_compounds" title="English compounds">
        English compounds
       </a>
       ). The longest German word verified to be actually in (albeit very limited) use is
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a href="/wiki/Rinderkennzeichnungs-_und_Rindfleischetikettierungs%C3%BCberwachungsaufgaben%C3%BCbertragungsgesetz" title="Rinderkennzeichnungs- und Rindfleischetikettierungsüberwachungsaufgabenübertragungsgesetz">
          Rindfleischetikettierungsüberwachungsaufgabenübertragungsgesetz
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       , which, literally translated, is "beef labelling supervision duty assignment law" [from
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Rind
        </span>
       </i>
       (cattle),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Fleisch
        </span>
       </i>
       (meat),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Etikettierung(s)
        </span>
       </i>
       (labelling),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Überwachung(s)
        </span>
       </i>
       (supervision),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Aufgaben
        </span>
       </i>
       (duties),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Übertragung(s)
        </span>
       </i>
       (assignment),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Gesetz
        </span>
       </i>
       (law)]. However, examples like this are perceived by native speakers as excessively bureaucratic, stylistically awkward or even satirical.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Verb_inflection">
        Verb inflection
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Verb inflection">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/German_verbs" title="German verbs">
        German verbs
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The inflection of standard German verbs includes:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        two main
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">
         conjugation
        </a>
        classes:
        <a href="/wiki/Germanic_weak_verb" title="Germanic weak verb">
         weak
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Germanic_strong_verb" title="Germanic strong verb">
         strong
        </a>
        (as in English). Additionally, there is a third class, known as mixed verbs, whose conjugation combines features of both the strong and weak patterns.
       </li>
       <li>
        three
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">
         persons
        </a>
        : first, second and third.
       </li>
       <li>
        two
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">
         numbers
        </a>
        : singular and plural.
       </li>
       <li>
        three
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">
         moods
        </a>
        :
        <a href="/wiki/Realis_mood" title="Realis mood">
         indicative
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">
         imperative
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Subjunctive_mood" title="Subjunctive mood">
         subjunctive
        </a>
        (in addition to
        <a href="/wiki/Infinitive" title="Infinitive">
         infinitive
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        two
        <a href="/wiki/Voice_(grammar)" title="Voice (grammar)">
         voices
        </a>
        : active and passive. The passive voice uses auxiliary verbs and is divisible into static and dynamic. Static forms show a constant state and use the verb ’’to be’’ (sein). Dynamic forms show an action and use the verb “to become’’ (werden).
       </li>
       <li>
        two
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">
         tenses
        </a>
        without auxiliary verbs (
        <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">
         present
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Preterite" title="Preterite">
         preterite
        </a>
        ) and four tenses constructed with auxiliary verbs (
        <a href="/wiki/Perfect_(grammar)" title="Perfect (grammar)">
         perfect
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Pluperfect" title="Pluperfect">
         pluperfect
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Future_tense" title="Future tense">
         future
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Future_perfect" title="Future perfect">
         future perfect
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        the distinction between
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">
         grammatical aspects
        </a>
        is rendered by combined use of subjunctive and/or preterite marking so the plain indicative voice uses neither of those two markers; the subjunctive by itself conveys secondhand information
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
           <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2012)">
            clarification needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
        ; subjunctive plus preterite marks the conditional state; and the preterite alone shows either plain indicative (in the past), or functions as a (literal) alternative for either second-hand-information or the conditional state of the verb, when necessary for clarity.
       </li>
       <li>
        the distinction between perfect and
        <a href="/wiki/Continuous_and_progressive_aspects" title="Continuous and progressive aspects">
         progressive aspect
        </a>
        is and has, at every stage of development, been a productive category of the older language and in nearly all documented dialects, but, strangely enough, it is now rigorously excluded from written usage in its present normalised form.
       </li>
       <li>
        disambiguation of completed vs. uncompleted forms is widely observed and regularly generated by common prefixes (
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/blicken#German" title="wikt:blicken">
           blicken
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        [to look],
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/erblicken#German" title="wikt:erblicken">
           erblicken
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        [to see – unrelated form:
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sehen#German" title="wikt:sehen">
           sehen
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        ]).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Verb_prefixes">
        Verb prefixes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Verb prefixes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The meaning of basic verbs can be expanded and sometimes radically changed through the use of a number of prefixes. Some prefixes have a specific meaning; the prefix
       <i>
        <b>
         <i>
          <span lang="de">
           zer-
          </span>
         </i>
        </b>
       </i>
       refers to destruction, as in
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <b>
          zer
         </b>
         reißen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to tear apart),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <b>
          zer
         </b>
         brechen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to break apart),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <b>
          zer
         </b>
         schneiden
        </span>
       </i>
       (to cut apart). Other prefixes have only the vaguest meaning in themselves;
       <i>
        <b>
         <i>
          <span lang="de">
           ver-
          </span>
         </i>
        </b>
       </i>
       is found in a number of verbs with a large variety of meanings, as in
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <b>
          ver
         </b>
         suchen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to try) from
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         suchen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to seek),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <b>
          ver
         </b>
         nehmen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to interrogate) from
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         nehmen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to take),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <b>
          ver
         </b>
         teilen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to distribute) from
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         teilen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to share),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <b>
          ver
         </b>
         stehen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to understand) from
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         stehen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to stand).
      </p>
      <p>
       Other examples include the following:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         haften
        </span>
       </i>
       (to stick),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <b>
          ver
         </b>
         haften
        </span>
       </i>
       (to detain);
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         kaufen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to buy),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <b>
          ver
         </b>
         kaufen
        </span>
       </i>
       (to sell);
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         hören
        </span>
       </i>
       (to hear),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <b>
          auf
         </b>
         hören
        </span>
       </i>
       (to cease);
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         fahren
        </span>
       </i>
       (to drive),
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <b>
          er
         </b>
         fahren
        </span>
       </i>
       (to experience).
      </p>
      <p>
       Many
       <a href="/wiki/German_verbs" title="German verbs">
        German verbs
       </a>
       have a separable prefix, often with an adverbial function. In
       <a href="/wiki/Finite_verb" title="Finite verb">
        finite verb
       </a>
       forms, it is split off and moved to the end of the clause and is hence considered by some to be a "resultative particle". For example,
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         mitgehen
        </span>
       </i>
       , meaning "to go along", would be split, giving
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Gehen Sie mit?
        </span>
       </i>
       (Literal: "Go you with?"; Idiomatic: "Are you going along?").
      </p>
      <p>
       Indeed, several
       <a href="/wiki/Parenthetical_referencing" title="Parenthetical referencing">
        parenthetical
       </a>
       clauses may occur between the prefix of a finite verb and its complement (ankommen = to arrive, er kam an = he arrived, er ist angekommen = he has arrived):
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Er
          <b>
           kam
          </b>
          am Freitagabend nach einem harten Arbeitstag und dem üblichen Ärger, der ihn schon seit Jahren immer wieder an seinem Arbeitsplatz plagt, mit fraglicher Freude auf ein Mahl, das seine Frau ihm, wie er hoffte, bereits aufgetischt hatte, endlich zu Hause
          <b>
           an
          </b>
          .
         </span>
        </i>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       A selectively literal translation of this example to illustrate the point might look like this:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        He "came" on Friday evening, after a hard day at work and the usual annoyances that had time and again been troubling him for years now at his workplace, with questionable joy, to a meal which, as he hoped, his wife had already put on the table, finally at home "on".
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Word_order">
        Word order
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Word order">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       German word order is generally with the
       <a href="/wiki/V2_word_order" title="V2 word order">
        V2 word order
       </a>
       restriction and also with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SOV_word_order" title="SOV word order">
        SOV word order
       </a>
       restriction for main
       <a href="/wiki/Clause" title="Clause">
        clauses
       </a>
       . For polar questions, exclamations and wishes, the
       <a href="/wiki/Finite_verb" title="Finite verb">
        finite verb
       </a>
       always has the first position. In subordinate clauses, the verb occurs at the very end.
      </p>
      <p>
       German requires for a verbal element (main verb or
       <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">
        auxiliary verb
       </a>
       ) to appear
       <a href="/wiki/V2_word_order" title="V2 word order">
        second in the sentence
       </a>
       . The verb is preceded by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topic%E2%80%93comment" title="Topic–comment">
        topic
       </a>
       of the sentence. The element in focus appears at the end of the sentence. For a sentence without an auxiliary, these are some possibilities:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Der alte Mann gab mir gestern das Buch.
         </span>
        </i>
        (The old man gave me yesterday the book; normal order)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Das Buch gab mir gestern der alte Mann.
         </span>
        </i>
        (The book gave [to] me yesterday the old man)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Das Buch gab der alte Mann mir gestern.
         </span>
        </i>
        (The book gave the old man [to] me yesterday)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Das Buch gab mir der alte Mann gestern.
         </span>
        </i>
        (The book gave [to] me the old man yesterday)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Gestern gab mir der alte Mann das Buch.
         </span>
        </i>
        (Yesterday gave [to] me the old man the book, normal order)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Mir gab der alte Mann das Buch gestern.
         </span>
        </i>
        ([To] me gave the old man the book yesterday (entailing: as for you, it was another date))
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The position of a noun in a German sentence has no bearing on its being a subject, an object or another argument. In a
       <a href="/wiki/Sentence_(linguistics)" title="Sentence (linguistics)">
        declarative sentence
       </a>
       in English, if the subject does not occur before the predicate, the sentence could well be misunderstood.
      </p>
      <p>
       However, German's flexibile word order allows one to emphasise specific words:
      </p>
      <p>
       Normal word order:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Der Direktor betrat gestern um 10 Uhr mit einem Schirm in der Hand sein Büro.
           </span>
          </i>
         </dd>
         <dd>
          The manager entered yesterday at 10 o'clock with an umbrella in the hand his office.
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Object in front:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Sein Büro betrat der Direktor gestern um 10 Uhr mit einem Schirm in der Hand.
           </span>
          </i>
         </dd>
         <dd>
          His office entered the manager yesterday at 10 o'clock with an umbrella in the hand.
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        The object
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Sein Büro
         </span>
        </i>
        (his office) is thus highlighted; it could be the topic of the next sentence.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Adverb of time in front:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Gestern betrat der Direktor um 10 Uhr mit einem Schirm in der Hand sein Büro. (aber heute ohne Schirm)
           </span>
          </i>
         </dd>
         <dd>
          Yesterday entered the manager at 10 o'clock with an umbrella in the hand his office. (but today without umbrella)
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Both time expressions in front:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Gestern um 10 Uhr betrat der Direktor mit einem Schirm in der Hand sein Büro
           </span>
          </i>
          .
         </dd>
         <dd>
          Yesterday at 10 o'clock entered the manager with an umbrella in the hand his office.
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        The full-time specification
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Gestern um 10 Uhr
         </span>
        </i>
        is highlighted.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Another possibility:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Gestern um 10 Uhr betrat der Direktor sein Büro mit einem Schirm in der Hand
           </span>
          </i>
          .
         </dd>
         <dd>
          Yesterday at 10 o'clock the manager entered his office with an umbrella in his hand.
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        Both the time specification and the fact he carried an umbrella are accentuated.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Swapped adverbs:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Der Direktor betrat mit einem Schirm in der Hand gestern um 10 Uhr sein Büro.
           </span>
          </i>
         </dd>
         <dd>
          The manager entered with an umbrella in the hand yesterday at 10 o'clock his office.
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        The phrase
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          mit einem Schirm in der Hand
         </span>
        </i>
        is highlighted.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Swapped object:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Der Direktor betrat gestern um 10 Uhr sein Büro mit einem Schirm in der Hand.
           </span>
          </i>
         </dd>
         <dd>
          The manager entered yesterday at 10 o'clock his office with an umbrella in his hand.
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        The time specification and the object
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          sein Büro
         </span>
        </i>
        (his office) are lightly accentuated.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The flexible word order also allows one to use language "tools" (such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poetic_meter" title="Poetic meter">
        poetic meter
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Figures_of_speech" title="Figures of speech">
        figures of speech
       </a>
       ) more freely.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Auxiliary_verbs">
        Auxiliary verbs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Auxiliary verbs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       When an
       <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">
        auxiliary verb
       </a>
       is present, it appears in second position, and the main verb appears at the end. This occurs notably in the creation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Perfect_(grammar)" title="Perfect (grammar)">
        perfect tense
       </a>
       . Many word orders are still possible:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Der alte Mann hat mir heute das Buch gegeben.
         </span>
        </i>
        (The old man has me today the book given.)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Das Buch hat der alte Mann mir heute gegeben.
         </span>
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         <b>
          The book
         </b>
        </i>
        has the old man me today given.)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Heute hat der alte Mann mir das Buch gegeben.
         </span>
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         <b>
          Today
         </b>
        </i>
        has the old man me the book given.)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The main verb may appear in first position to put stress on the action itself. The
       <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">
        auxiliary verb
       </a>
       is still in second position.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Gegeben hat mir der alte Mann das Buch heute.
         </span>
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         <b>
          Given
         </b>
        </i>
        has me the old man the book '
        <b>
         today'
        </b>
        .) The bare fact that the book has been given is emphasized, as well as 'today'.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modal_verbs">
        Modal verbs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Modal verbs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Sentences using modal verbs place the infinitive at the end. For example, the English sentence "Should he go home?" would be rearranged in German to say "Should he (to) home go?" (
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Soll er nach Hause gehen?
        </span>
       </i>
       ). Thus, in sentences with several subordinate or relative clauses, the infinitives are clustered at the end. Compare the similar clustering of prepositions in the following (highly contrived) English sentence: "What did you bring that book that I do not like to be read to out of up for?"
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Multiple_infinitives">
        Multiple infinitives
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Multiple infinitives">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       German subordinate clauses have all verbs clustered at the end. Given that auxiliaries encode
       <a href="/wiki/Future" title="Future">
        future
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Passive_voice" title="Passive voice">
        passive
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Modality_(semiotics)" title="Modality (semiotics)">
        modality
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Perfect_(grammar)" title="Perfect (grammar)">
        perfect
       </a>
       , very long chains of verbs at the end of the sentence can occur. In these constructions, the past participle in
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         ge-
        </span>
       </i>
       is often replaced by the infinitive.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Man nimmt an, dass der Deserteur wohl erschossen
         </span>
         <sub>
          V
         </sub>
         <span lang="de">
          worden
         </span>
         <sub>
          psv
         </sub>
         <span lang="de">
          sein
         </span>
         <sub>
          perf
         </sub>
         <span lang="de">
          soll
         </span>
         <sub>
          mod
         </sub>
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        One suspects that the deserter probably shot become be should.
       </dd>
       <dd>
        ("It is suspected that the deserter probably had been shot")
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Er wusste nicht, dass der Agent einen Nachschlüssel hatte machen lassen
           </span>
          </i>
         </dd>
         <dd>
          He knew not that the agent a picklock had make let
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Er wusste nicht, dass der Agent einen Nachschlüssel machen lassen hatte
           </span>
          </i>
         </dd>
         <dd>
          He knew not that the agent a picklock make let had
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        ("He did not know that the agent had had a picklock made")
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The order at the end of such strings is subject to variation, but the latter version is unusual.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">
        Vocabulary
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Duden_25Auflage.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="701" data-file-width="500" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Duden_25Auflage.JPG/170px-Duden_25Auflage.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Duden_25Auflage.JPG/255px-Duden_25Auflage.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Duden_25Auflage.JPG/340px-Duden_25Auflage.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Duden_25Auflage.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Duden" title="Duden">
           Duden
          </a>
         </i>
         dictionary.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OeWB_42Aufl.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/OeWB_42Aufl.jpg/170px-OeWB_42Aufl.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/OeWB_42Aufl.jpg/255px-OeWB_42Aufl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/OeWB_42Aufl.jpg/340px-OeWB_42Aufl.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OeWB_42Aufl.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <span lang="de">
           <a href="/wiki/%C3%96sterreichisches_W%C3%B6rterbuch" title="Österreichisches Wörterbuch">
            Österreichisches Wörterbuch
           </a>
          </span>
         </i>
         , Austrian Dictionary.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Most German vocabulary is derived from the Germanic branch of the European language family.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This was cited in the lead to a source that said nothing of the sort. Also, 'derives vocabulary from the Germanic branch' is problematic, as technically English is a member of is branch (so the 'however' in the following sentence makes no sense). Assuming what is meant is that the majority of agerman words are descended from older German words and German has historically had a relatively small number of foreign loanwords, this is probably correct, but it still needs a source. (July 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       However, there is a significant amount of loanwords from other languages, in particular from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">
        Latin
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       , French
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and most recently English.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the early 19th century,
       <a href="/wiki/Joachim_Heinrich_Campe" title="Joachim Heinrich Campe">
        Joachim Heinrich Campe
       </a>
       estimated that one fifth of the total German vocabulary was of French or Latin origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Latin words were already imported into the predecessor of the German language during the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman Empire
       </a>
       and underwent all the characteristic phonetic changes in German. Their origin is thus no longer recognizable for most speakers (e.g.
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Pforte
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Tafel
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Mauer
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Käse
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Köln
        </span>
       </i>
       from Latin
       <i>
        <span lang="la">
         porta
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="la">
         tabula
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="la">
         murus
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="la">
         caseus
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="la">
         Colonia
        </span>
       </i>
       ). Borrowing from Latin continued after the fall of the Roman Empire during Christianization, mediated by the church and monasteries. Another important influx of Latin words can be observed during
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">
        Renaissance humanism
       </a>
       . In a scholarly context, the borrowings from Latin have continued until today, in the last few decades often indirectly through borrowings from English. During the 15th to 17th centuries, the influence of Italian was great, leading to many Italian loanwords in the fields of architecture, finance, and music. The influence of the French language in the 17th to 19th centuries resulted in an even greater import of French words. The English influence was already present in the 19th century, but it did not become dominant until the second half of the 20th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       At the same time, the effectiveness of the German language in forming equivalents for foreign words from its inherited Germanic stem repertory is great.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Thus,
       <a href="/wiki/Notker_Labeo" title="Notker Labeo">
        Notker Labeo
       </a>
       was able to translate Aristotelian treatises in pure (Old High) German in the decades after the year 1000. The tradition of loan translation was revitalized in the 18th century, with linguists like
       <a href="/wiki/Joachim_Heinrich_Campe" title="Joachim Heinrich Campe">
        Joachim Heinrich Campe
       </a>
       , who introduced close to 300 words that are still used in modern German. Even today, there are movements that try to promote the
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ersatz" title="Ersatz">
          Ersatz
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       (substitution) of foreign words deemed unnecessary with German alternatives.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is claimed that this would also help in spreading modern or scientific notions among the less educated and as well democratise public life.
      </p>
      <p>
       As in English, there are many pairs of
       <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">
        synonyms
       </a>
       due to the enrichment of the Germanic vocabulary with
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loanwords
       </a>
       from Latin and Latinized Greek. These words often have different connotations from their Germanic counterparts and are usually perceived as more scholarly.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Historie
         </span>
        </i>
        – "historical", (
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Geschichte, geschichtlich
         </span>
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Humanität
         </span>
        </i>
        – "humaneness", (
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Menschlichkeit
         </span>
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Millennium
         </span>
        </i>
        – "millennium", (
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Jahrtausend
         </span>
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Perzeption
         </span>
        </i>
        – "perception", (
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Wahrnehmung
         </span>
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Vokabular
         </span>
        </i>
        – "vocabulary", (
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Wortschatz
         </span>
        </i>
        )
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The size of the vocabulary of German is difficult to estimate. The
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a href="/wiki/Deutsches_W%C3%B6rterbuch" title="Deutsches Wörterbuch">
          Deutsches Wörterbuch
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        The German Dictionary
       </i>
       ) initiated by
       <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Grimm" title="Jacob Grimm">
        Jacob
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Grimm" title="Wilhelm Grimm">
        Wilhelm Grimm
       </a>
       already contained over 330,000 headwords in its first edition. The modern German scientific vocabulary is estimated at nine million words and word groups (based on the analysis of 35 million sentences of a
       <a href="/wiki/Text_corpus" title="Text corpus">
        corpus
       </a>
       in Leipzig, which as of July 2003 included 500 million words in total).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Duden" title="Duden">
        Duden
       </a>
       is the
       <i>
        de facto
       </i>
       official
       <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">
        dictionary
       </a>
       of the German language, first published by
       <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Duden" title="Konrad Duden">
        Konrad Duden
       </a>
       in 1880. The Duden is updated regularly, with new editions appearing every four or five years. As of August 2013
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it is in its 26th edition and in 12 volumes, each covering different aspects such as
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loanwords
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">
        etymology
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation" title="Pronunciation">
        pronunciation
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synonyms" title="Synonyms">
        synonyms
       </a>
       , and so forth.
       <br/>
       The first of these volumes,
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Die deutsche Rechtschreibung
        </span>
       </i>
       (English:
       <span lang="en">
        German Orthography
       </span>
       ), has long been the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Usage_dictionary" title="Usage dictionary">
        prescriptive
       </a>
       source for the spelling of German. The
       <i>
        Duden
       </i>
       has become the bible of the German language, being the definitive set of rules regarding grammar, spelling and usage of German.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a href="/wiki/%C3%96sterreichisches_W%C3%B6rterbuch" title="Österreichisches Wörterbuch">
          Österreichisches Wörterbuch
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ("Austrian Dictionary"), abbreviated
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96WB" title="ÖWB">
          ÖWB
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       , is the official
       <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">
        dictionary
       </a>
       of the German language in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Austria" title="Republic of Austria">
        Republic of Austria
       </a>
       . It is edited by a group of linguists under the authority of the Austrian
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Ministry_of_Education,_Arts_and_Culture" title="Federal Ministry of Education, Arts and Culture">
        Federal Ministry of Education, Arts and Culture
       </a>
       (German:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Bundesministerium für Unterricht, Kunst und Kultur
        </span>
       </i>
       ). It is the Austrian counterpart to the German
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Duden" title="Duden">
         Duden
        </a>
       </i>
       and contains a number of terms unique to
       <a href="/wiki/Austrian_German" title="Austrian German">
        Austrian German
       </a>
       or more frequently used or differently pronounced there.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A considerable amount of this "Austrian" vocabulary is also common in
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Germany" title="Southern Germany">
        Southern Germany
       </a>
       , especially
       <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">
        Bavaria
       </a>
       , and some of it is used in
       <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">
        Switzerland
       </a>
       as well. The most recent edition is the 42nd from 2012. Since the 39th edition from 2001 the orthography of the
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         ÖWB
        </span>
       </i>
       was adjusted to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_spelling_reform_of_1996" title="German spelling reform of 1996">
        German spelling reform of 1996
       </a>
       . The dictionary is also officially used in the
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italian
       </a>
       province of
       <a href="/wiki/South_Tyrol" title="South Tyrol">
        South Tyrol
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="English.E2.80.93German_cognates">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="English–German_cognates">
        English–German cognates
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: English–German cognates">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       This is a selection of
       <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">
        cognates
       </a>
       in both English and German. Instead of the usual infinitive ending
       <i>
        -en
       </i>
       German verbs are indicated by a hyphen "-" after their stems. Words that are written with capital letters in German are nouns.
      </p>
