      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">
        Orthography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Orthography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/German_orthography" title="German orthography">
        German orthography
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_braille" title="German braille">
        German braille
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1995_Schulschrift_2._Schraegschrift.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2800" data-file-width="1702" height="362" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/1995_Schulschrift_2._Schraegschrift.jpg/220px-1995_Schulschrift_2._Schraegschrift.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/1995_Schulschrift_2._Schraegschrift.jpg/330px-1995_Schulschrift_2._Schraegschrift.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/1995_Schulschrift_2._Schraegschrift.jpg/440px-1995_Schulschrift_2._Schraegschrift.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1995_Schulschrift_2._Schraegschrift.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_alphabet" title="German alphabet">
          German alphabet
         </a>
         , one of Austria's elementary school handwriting programs
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vereinfachte_Ausgangsschrift.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="800" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Vereinfachte_Ausgangsschrift.png/220px-Vereinfachte_Ausgangsschrift.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Vereinfachte_Ausgangsschrift.png/330px-Vereinfachte_Ausgangsschrift.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Vereinfachte_Ausgangsschrift.png/440px-Vereinfachte_Ausgangsschrift.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vereinfachte_Ausgangsschrift.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_alphabet" title="German alphabet">
          German alphabet
         </a>
         , elementary school handwriting program in some West German states
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       German is written in the Latin alphabet. In addition to the 26 standard letters, German has three vowels with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umlaut_(diacritic)" title="Umlaut (diacritic)">
        Umlaut
       </a>
       , namely
       <i>
        ä
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        ö
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ü
       </i>
       , as well as the eszett or
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scharfes_s" title="Scharfes s">
          scharfes s
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       (sharp s):
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/%C3%9F" title="ß">
         ß
        </a>
       </i>
       . In Switzerland and Liechtenstein,
       <i>
        ss
       </i>
       is used instead of
       <i>
        ß
       </i>
       . Since
       <i>
        ß
       </i>
       can never occur at the beginning of a word, it has no traditional uppercase form.
      </p>
      <p>
       Written texts in German are easily recognisable as such by distinguishing features such as
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_umlaut" title="Germanic umlaut">
        umlauts
       </a>
       and certain
       <a href="/wiki/German_orthography" title="German orthography">
        orthographical
       </a>
       features – German is the only major language that capitalizes all nouns, a relic of a widespread practice in Northern Europe in the early modern era (including English for a while, in the 1700s) – and the frequent occurrence of long compounds. The longest German word that has been published is
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a href="/wiki/Donaudampfschiffahrtselektrizit%C3%A4tenhauptbetriebswerkbauunterbeamtengesellschaft" title="Donaudampfschiffahrtselektrizitätenhauptbetriebswerkbauunterbeamtengesellschaft">
          Donaudampfschiffahrtselektrizitätenhauptbetriebswerkbauunterbeamtengesellschaft
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       made of 79 characters. Because legibility and convenience set certain boundaries, compounds consisting of more than three or four nouns are almost exclusively found in humorous contexts. (In contrast, although English can also string nouns together, it usually separates the nouns with spaces. For example, "toilet bowl cleaner".)
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Present">
        Present
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Present">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Before the
       <a href="/wiki/German_orthography_reform_of_1996" title="German orthography reform of 1996">
        German orthography reform of 1996
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        ß
       </i>
       replaced
       <i>
        ss
       </i>
       after
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">
        long vowels
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        diphthongs
       </a>
       and before consonants, word-, or partial-word-endings. In reformed spelling,
       <i>
        ß
       </i>
       replaces
       <i>
        ss
       </i>
       only after long vowels and diphthongs.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since there is no traditional capital form of
       <i>
        ß
       </i>
       , it was replaced by
       <i>
        SS
       </i>
       when capitalization was required. For example,
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Maßband
        </span>
       </i>
       (tape measure) became
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         MASSBAND
        </span>
       </i>
       in capitals. An exception was the use of ß in legal documents and forms when capitalizing names. To avoid confusion with similar names, lower case
       <i>
        ß
       </i>
       was maintained (so, "
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         KREßLEIN
        </span>
       </i>
       " instead of "
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         KRESSLEIN
        </span>
       </i>
       ").
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capital_%C3%9F" title="Capital ß">
        Capital ß
       </a>
       (ẞ) was ultimately adopted into German orthography in 2017, ending a long orthographic debate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Umlaut vowels (ä, ö, ü) are commonly transcribed with ae, oe, and ue if the umlauts are not available on the keyboard or other medium used. In the same manner ß can be transcribed as ss. Some
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operating_systems" title="Operating systems">
        operating systems
       </a>
       use key sequences to extend the set of possible characters to include, amongst other things, umlauts; in
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">
        Microsoft Windows
       </a>
       this is done using
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alt_codes" title="Alt codes">
        Alt codes
       </a>
       . German readers understand these transcriptions (although they appear unusual), but they are avoided if the regular umlauts are available because they are a makeshift, not proper spelling. (In Westphalia and Schleswig-Holstein, city and family names exist where the extra e has a vowel lengthening effect, e.g.
       <i>
        Raesfeld
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">
         [ˈraːsfɛlt]
        </a>
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        Coesfeld
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈkoːsfɛlt]
       </span>
       and
       <i>
        Itzehoe
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɪtsəˈhoː]
       </span>
       , but this use of the letter e after a/o/u does not occur in the present-day spelling of words other than
       <a href="/wiki/Proper_noun" title="Proper noun">
        proper nouns
       </a>
       .)
      </p>
      <table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div class="center">
          <div class="floatnone">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </div>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
         <div class="haudio">
          <div style="padding:4px 0">
           <a href="/wiki/File:German_alphabet-2.ogg" title="File:German alphabet-2.ogg">
            German alphabet
           </a>
          </div>
          <div>
           <div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px">
            <audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="27.747845804989" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="German_alphabet-2.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px">
             <source data-bandwidth="82725" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (83 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/German_alphabet-2.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/>
             <source data-bandwidth="136896" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/92/German_alphabet-2.ogg/German_alphabet-2.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/>
             <track data-dir="ltr" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="TimedText:German_alphabet-2.ogg.en.srt" kind="subtitles" label="English (en) subtitles" src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?title=TimedText:German_alphabet-2.ogg.en.srt&amp;action=raw&amp;ctype=text%2Fx-srt" srclang="en" type="text/x-srt"/>
            </audio>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
           (Listen to a German speaker recite the alphabet in German)
          </div>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">
         <hr/>
         <span>
          <i>
           Problems playing this file? See
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">
            media help
           </a>
           .
          </i>
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       There is no general agreement on where letters with umlauts occur in the sorting sequence. Telephone directories treat them by replacing them with the base vowel followed by an e. Some dictionaries sort each umlauted vowel as a separate letter after the base vowel, but more commonly words with umlauts are ordered immediately after the same word without umlauts. As an example in a
       <a href="/wiki/Telephone_directory" title="Telephone directory">
        telephone book
       </a>
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Ärzte
        </span>
       </i>
       occurs after
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Adressenverlage
        </span>
       </i>
       but before
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Anlagenbauer
        </span>
       </i>
       (because Ä is replaced by Ae). In a dictionary
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Ärzte
        </span>
       </i>
       comes after
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Arzt
        </span>
       </i>
       , but in some dictionaries
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Ärzte
        </span>
       </i>
       and all other words starting with
       <i>
        Ä
       </i>
       may occur after all words starting with
       <i>
        A
       </i>
       . In some older dictionaries or indexes, initial
       <i>
        Sch
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        St
       </i>
       are treated as separate letters and are listed as separate entries after
       <i>
        S
       </i>
       , but they are usually treated as S+C+H and S+T.
      </p>
      <p>
       Written German also typically uses an alternative opening inverted comma (
       <a href="/wiki/Quotation_mark" title="Quotation mark">
        quotation mark
       </a>
       ) as in
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         „Guten Morgen!“
        </span>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Past">
        Past
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Past">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fraktur-ru.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1306" data-file-width="1336" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Fraktur-ru.jpg/220px-Fraktur-ru.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Fraktur-ru.jpg/330px-Fraktur-ru.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Fraktur-ru.jpg/440px-Fraktur-ru.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fraktur-ru.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A Russian dictionary from 1931, showing the "German alphabet" – the 3rd and 4th columns of each half are
         <a href="/wiki/Fraktur" title="Fraktur">
          Fraktur
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Kurrent" title="Kurrent">
          Kurrent
         </a>
         respectively, with the footnote explaining
         <a href="/wiki/Typographic_ligature" title="Typographic ligature">
          ligatures
         </a>
         used in Fraktur.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2nd_Orthographic_Conference_(German)" title="2nd Orthographic Conference (German)">
        2nd Orthographic Conference (German)
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Antiqua%E2%80%93Fraktur_dispute" title="Antiqua–Fraktur dispute">
        Antiqua–Fraktur dispute
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/German_orthography_reform_of_1944" title="German orthography reform of 1944">
        German orthography reform of 1944
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Until the early 20th century, German was mostly printed in
       <a href="/wiki/Blackletter" title="Blackletter">
        blackletter
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Typeface" title="Typeface">
        typefaces
       </a>
       (mostly in
       <a href="/wiki/Fraktur" title="Fraktur">
        Fraktur
       </a>
       , but also in
       <a href="/wiki/Schwabacher" title="Schwabacher">
        Schwabacher
       </a>
       ) and written in corresponding
       <a href="/wiki/Penmanship" title="Penmanship">
        handwriting
       </a>
       (for example
       <a href="/wiki/Kurrent" title="Kurrent">
        Kurrent
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%BCtterlin" title="Sütterlin">
        Sütterlin
       </a>
       ). These variants of the Latin alphabet are very different from the serif or
       <a href="/wiki/Sans-serif" title="Sans-serif">
        sans-serif
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Antiqua_(typeface_class)" title="Antiqua (typeface class)">
        Antiqua
       </a>
       typefaces used today, and the handwritten forms in particular are difficult for the untrained to read. The printed forms, however, were claimed by some to be more readable when used for
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic languages
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (Often, foreign names in a text were printed in an Antiqua typeface even though the rest of the text was in Fraktur.) The
       <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">
        Nazis
       </a>
       initially promoted Fraktur and Schwabacher because they were considered
       <a href="/wiki/Aryan" title="Aryan">
        Aryan
       </a>
       , but they abolished them in 1941, claiming that these letters were Jewish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is also believed that the Nazi régime had banned this script as they realized that Fraktur would inhibit communication in the territories occupied during
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Fraktur script however remains present in everyday life in pub signs, beer brands and other forms of advertisement, where it is used to convey a certain rusticality and antiquity.
      </p>
      <p>
       A proper use of the
       <a href="/wiki/Long_s" title="Long s">
        long s
       </a>
       , (
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         langes s
        </span>
       </i>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Long_s" title="Long s">
        ſ
       </a>
       , is essential for writing German text in
       <a href="/wiki/Fraktur" title="Fraktur">
        Fraktur
       </a>
       typefaces. Many
       <a href="/wiki/Antiqua_(typeface_class)" title="Antiqua (typeface class)">
        Antiqua
       </a>
       typefaces include the
       <a href="/wiki/Long_s" title="Long s">
        long s
       </a>
       also. A specific set of rules applies for the use of long s in German text, but nowadays it is rarely used in Antiqua typesetting. Any lower case "s" at the beginning of a syllable would be a long s, as opposed to a terminal s or short s (the more common variation of the letter s), which marks the end of a syllable; for example, in differentiating between the words
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Wachſtube
        </span>
       </i>
       (guard-house) and
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Wachstube
        </span>
       </i>
       (tube of polish/wax). One can easily decide which "s" to use by appropriate hyphenation, (
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Wach-ſtube
        </span>
       </i>
       vs.
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Wachs-tube
        </span>
       </i>
       ). The long s only appears in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_case" title="Lower case">
        lower case
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Reform_of_1996">
        Reform of 1996
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Reform of 1996">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/German_orthography_reform_of_1996" title="German orthography reform of 1996">
        German orthography reform of 1996
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The orthography reform of 1996 led to public controversy and considerable dispute. The states (
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Bundesländer
        </span>
       </i>
       ) of
       <a href="/wiki/North_Rhine-Westphalia" title="North Rhine-Westphalia">
        North Rhine-Westphalia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">
        Bavaria
       </a>
       would not accept it. The dispute landed at one point in the highest court, which made a short issue of it, claiming that the states had to decide for themselves and that only in schools could the reform be made the official rule – everybody else could continue writing as they had learned it. After 10 years, without any intervention by the federal parliament, a major revision was installed in 2006, just in time for the coming school year. In 2007, some traditional spellings were finally invalidated, whereas in 2008, on the other hand, many of the old
       <a href="/wiki/Comma" title="Comma">
        comma
       </a>
       rules were again put in force.
      </p>
      <p>
       The most noticeable change was probably in the use of the letter
       <i>
        ß
       </i>
       , called
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         scharfes s
        </span>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Sharp S
       </i>
       ) or
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         ess-zett
        </span>
       </i>
       (pronounced
       <i>
        ess-tsett
       </i>
       ). Traditionally, this letter was used in three situations:
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        After a long vowel or vowel combination,
       </li>
       <li>
        Before a
        <i>
         t
        </i>
        , and
       </li>
       <li>
        At the end of a syllable
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       Thus
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Füße
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         paßt
        </span>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         daß
        </span>
       </i>
       . Currently only the first rule is in effect, thus
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Füße
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         passt
        </span>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         dass
        </span>
       </i>
       . The word
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Fuß
        </span>
       </i>
       'foot' has the letter
       <i>
        ß
       </i>
       because it contains a long vowel, even though that letter occurs at the end of a syllable. The logic of this change is that an 'ß' is a single letter whereas 'ss' obviously are two letters, so the same distinction applies as for instance between the words
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         den
        </span>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         denn
        </span>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_phonology" title="German phonology">
        German phonology
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">
        Vowels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Vowels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px">
         <audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="847.27672916667" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="De-Faust_-_Der_Tragödie_erster_Teil-Prolog_und_Auf_dem_Theater-wikisource.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_1" preload="none" style="width:220px">
          <source data-bandwidth="92666" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (93 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e3/De-Faust_-_Der_Trag%C3%B6die_erster_Teil-Prolog_und_Auf_dem_Theater-wikisource.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/>
          <source data-bandwidth="189816" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e3/De-Faust_-_Der_Trag%C3%B6die_erster_Teil-Prolog_und_Auf_dem_Theater-wikisource.ogg/De-Faust_-_Der_Trag%C3%B6die_erster_Teil-Prolog_und_Auf_dem_Theater-wikisource.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/>
         </audio>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:De-Faust_-_Der_Trag%C3%B6die_erster_Teil-Prolog_und_Auf_dem_Theater-wikisource.ogg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Spoken German in
         <a href="/wiki/Goethe%27s_Faust" title="Goethe's Faust">
          Goethe's Faust
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In German, vowels (excluding diphthongs; see below) are either
       <i>
        short
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        long
       </i>
       , as follows:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
        </th>
        <th>
         A
        </th>
        <th>
         Ä
        </th>
        <th>
         E
        </th>
        <th>
         I
        </th>
        <th>
         O
        </th>
        <th>
         Ö
        </th>
        <th>
         U
        </th>
        <th>
         Ü
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         short
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /a/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɛ/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɛ/, /ə/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɪ/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɔ/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /œ/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ʊ/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ʏ/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         long
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /aː/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɛː/, /eː/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /eː/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /iː/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /oː/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /øː/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /uː/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /yː/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Short
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛ/
       </span>
       is realized as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɛ]
       </span>
       in stressed syllables (including
       <a href="/wiki/Secondary_stress" title="Secondary stress">
        secondary stress
       </a>
       ), but as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ə]
       </span>
       in unstressed syllables. Note that stressed short
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛ/
       </span>
       can be spelled either with
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       or with
       <i>
        ä
       </i>
       (for instance,
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         hätte
        </span>
       </i>
       "would have" and
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Kette
        </span>
       </i>
       "chain" rhyme). In general, the short vowels are open and the long vowels are close. The one exception is the open
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛː/
       </span>
       sound of long
       <i>
        Ä
       </i>
       ; in some varieties of standard German,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛː/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /eː/
       </span>
       have merged into
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [eː]
       </span>
       , removing this anomaly. In that case, pairs like
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Bären/Beeren
        </span>
       </i>
       'bears/berries' or
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Ähre/Ehre
        </span>
       </i>
       'spike (of wheat)/honour' become homophonous (see:
       <a href="/wiki/Captain_Bluebear" title="Captain Bluebear">
        Captain Bluebear
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       In many varieties of standard German, an unstressed
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛr/
       </span>
       is not pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ər]
       </span>
       , but vocalised to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɐ]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Whether any particular vowel letter represents the long or short phoneme is not completely predictable, although the following regularities exist:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        If a vowel (other than
        <i>
         i
        </i>
        ) is at the end of a syllable or followed by a single consonant, it is usually pronounced long (e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Hof
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [hoːf]
        </span>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        If a vowel is followed by
        <i>
         h
        </i>
        or if an
        <i>
         i
        </i>
        is followed by an
        <i>
         e
        </i>
        , it is long.
       </li>
       <li>
        If the vowel is followed by a double consonant (e.g.
        <i>
         ff
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         ss
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         tt
        </i>
        ),
        <i>
         ck
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         tz
        </i>
        or a
        <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">
         consonant cluster
        </a>
        (e.g.
        <i>
         st
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         nd
        </i>
        ), it is nearly always short (e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          hoffen
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈhɔfən]
        </span>
        ). Double consonants are used only for this function of marking preceding vowels as short; the consonant itself is never pronounced lengthened or doubled, in other words this is not a
        <a href="/wiki/Feeding_order" title="Feeding order">
         feeding order
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">
         gemination
        </a>
        and then
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vowel_shortening" title="Vowel shortening">
         vowel shortening
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Both of these rules have exceptions (e.g.
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         hat
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [hat]
       </span>
       "has" is short despite the first rule;
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Mond
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [moːnt]
       </span>
       , "
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        moon
       </a>
       " is long despite the second rule). For an
       <i>
        i
       </i>
       that is neither in the combination
       <i>
        ie
       </i>
       (making it long) nor followed by a double consonant or cluster (making it short), there is no general rule. In some cases, there are regional differences: In central Germany (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hessen" title="Hessen">
        Hessen
       </a>
       ), the
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Noun#Proper_nouns_and_common_nouns" title="Noun">
        proper name
       </a>
       "Hoffmann" is pronounced long, whereas most other Germans would pronounce it short; the same applies to the
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       in the geographical name "
       <a href="/wiki/Mecklenburg" title="Mecklenburg">
        Mecklenburg
       </a>
       " for people in that region. The word
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Städte
        </span>
       </i>
       "cities", is pronounced with a short vowel
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈʃtɛtə]
       </span>
       by some (
       <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hofer" title="Jan Hofer">
        Jan Hofer
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Das_Erste" title="Das Erste">
        ARD Television
       </a>
       ) and with a long vowel
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈʃtɛːtə]
       </span>
       by others (
       <a href="/wiki/Marietta_Slomka" title="Marietta Slomka">
        Marietta Slomka
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/ZDF" title="ZDF">
        ZDF
       </a>
       Television). Finally, a vowel followed by
       <i>
        ch
       </i>
       can be short (
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Fach
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [fax]
       </span>
       "compartment",
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Küche
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈkʏçə]
       </span>
       "kitchen") or long (
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Suche
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈzuːxə]
       </span>
       "search",
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Bücher
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈbyːçɐ]
       </span>
       "books") almost at random. Thus,
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Lache
        </span>
       </i>
       is homographous between
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [laːxə]
       </span>
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Lache
        </span>
       </i>
       "puddle" and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [laxə]
       </span>
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Lache
        </span>
       </i>
       "manner of laughing" (colloquial) or
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         lache!
        </span>
       </i>
       "laugh!" (imperative).
      </p>
      <p>
       German vowels can form the following digraphs (in writing) and diphthongs (in pronunciation); note that the pronunciation of some of them (ei, äu, eu) is very different from what one would expect when considering the component letters:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         spelling
        </th>
        <td>
         ai, ei, ay, ey
        </td>
        <td>
         au
        </td>
        <td>
         äu, eu
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         pronunciation
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /aɪ̯/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /aʊ̯/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɔʏ̯/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Additionally, the digraph
       <i>
        ie
       </i>
       generally represents the phoneme
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /iː/
       </span>
       , which is not a diphthong. In many varieties, an
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /r/
       </span>
       at the end of a syllable is vocalised. However, a sequence of a vowel followed by such a vocalised
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /r/
       </span>
       is not a phonemic diphthong:
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Bär
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [bɛːɐ̯]
       </span>
       "bear",
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         er
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [eːɐ̯]
       </span>
       "he",
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         wir
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [viːɐ̯]
       </span>
       "we",
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Tor
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [toːɐ̯]
       </span>
       "gate",
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         kurz
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kʊɐ̯ts]
       </span>
       "short",
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Wörter
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [vœɐ̯tɐ]
       </span>
       "words".
      </p>
      <p>
       In most varieties of standard German, syllables that begin with a vowel are preceded by a
       <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">
        glottal stop
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʔ]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">
        Consonants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Consonants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       With approximately 25 phonemes, the German consonant system exhibits an average number of consonants in comparison with other languages. One of the more noteworthy ones is the unusual
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affricate" title="Affricate">
        affricate
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /p͡f/
       </span>
       . The consonant inventory of the standard language is shown below.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">
       <tr>
        <th>
        </th>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Labial_consonant" title="Labial consonant">
          Labial
         </a>
        </th>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_consonant" title="Alveolar consonant">
          Alveolar
         </a>
        </th>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Palatal_consonant" title="Palatal consonant">
          Palatal
         </a>
        </th>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">
          Velar
