          Uvular
         </a>
        </th>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Glottal_consonant" title="Glottal consonant">
          Glottal
         </a>
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasal_stop" title="Nasal stop">
          Nasal
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          m
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          n
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ŋ
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plosive_consonant" title="Plosive consonant">
          Plosive
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          p
         </span>
         <sup>
          3
         </sup>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          b
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          t
         </span>
         <sup>
          3
         </sup>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          d
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          k
         </span>
         <sup>
          3
         </sup>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ɡ
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" title="Affricate consonant">
          Affricate
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          pf
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ts
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          tʃ
         </span>
         (
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          dʒ
         </span>
         )
         <sup>
          4
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">
          Fricative
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          f
         </span>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          v
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          s
         </span>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          z
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ʃ
         </span>
         (
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ʒ
         </span>
         )
         <sup>
          4
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          x
         </span>
         <sup>
          1
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          (ʁ)
         </span>
         <sup>
          2
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          h
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Trill_consonant" title="Trill consonant">
          Trill
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          r
         </span>
         <sup>
          2
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          (ʀ)
         </span>
         <sup>
          2
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Approximant_consonant" title="Approximant consonant">
          Approximant
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          l
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          j
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /x/
        </span>
        has two allophones,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [x]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ç]
        </span>
        , after back and front vowels, respectively.
       </li>
       <li>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /r/
        </span>
        has three allophones in free variation:
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [r]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʁ]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʀ]
        </span>
        . In the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syllable_coda" title="Syllable coda">
         syllable coda
        </a>
        , the allophone
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɐ]
        </span>
        is found in many varieties.
       </li>
       <li>
        <sup>
         3
        </sup>
        The voiceless stops
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /p/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /t/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /k/
        </span>
        are
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspiration_(phonetics)" title="Aspiration (phonetics)">
         aspirated
        </a>
        except when preceded by a
        <a href="/wiki/Sibilant" title="Sibilant">
         sibilant
        </a>
        , exactly as in English.
       </li>
       <li>
        <sup>
         4
        </sup>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /d͡ʒ/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʒ/
        </span>
        occur only in words of foreign (usually English or French) origin.
       </li>
       <li>
        Where a stressed syllable has an initial vowel, it is preceded by
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʔ]
        </span>
        . As its presence is predictable from context,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʔ]
        </span>
        is not considered a phoneme.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Consonant_spellings">
        Consonant spellings
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Consonant spellings">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         c
        </b>
        standing by itself is not a German letter. In borrowed words, it is usually pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [t͡s]
        </span>
        (before ä, äu, e, i, ö, ü, y) or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [k]
        </span>
        (before a, o, u, and consonants). The combination
        <b>
         ck
        </b>
        is, as in English, used to indicate that the preceding vowel is short.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         ch
        </b>
        occurs often and is pronounced either
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ç]
        </span>
        (after ä, ai, äu, e, ei, eu, i, ö, ü and consonants; in the diminutive suffix
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          -chen
         </span>
        </i>
        ; and at the beginning of a word),
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [x]
        </span>
        (after a, au, o, u), or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [k]
        </span>
        at the beginning of a word before a, o, u and consonants.
        <b>
         Ch
        </b>
        never occurs at the beginning of an originally German word. In borrowed words with initial Ch before front vowels (
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Chemie
         </span>
        </i>
        "chemistry" etc.),
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ç]
        </span>
        is considered standard. However, Upper Germans and Franconians (in the geographical sense) replace it with
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [k]
        </span>
        , as German as a whole does before darker vowels and consonants such as in
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Charakter
         </span>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Christentum
         </span>
        </i>
        . Middle Germans (except Franconians) will borrow a
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʃ]
        </span>
        from the French model. Both agree in considering each other's variant,
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
           <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2012)">
            clarification needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
        and Upper Germans also the standard in
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ç]
        </span>
        , as particularly awkward and unusual.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         dsch
        </b>
        is pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [d͡ʒ]
        </span>
        (e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Dschungel
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ˈd͡ʒʊŋəl/
        </span>
        "jungle") but appears in a few
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loanwords" title="Loanwords">
         loanwords
        </a>
        only.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         f
        </b>
        is pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [f]
        </span>
        as in "
        <i>
         f
        </i>
        ather".
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         h
        </b>
        is pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [h]
        </span>
        as in "
        <i>
         h
        </i>
        ome" at the beginning of a syllable. After a vowel it is silent and only lengthens the vowel (e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Reh
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʁeː]
        </span>
        =
        <a href="/wiki/Roe_deer" title="Roe deer">
         roe deer
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         j
        </b>
        is pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [j]
        </span>
        in Germanic words (
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Jahr
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [jaːɐ]
        </span>
        ) (like "y" in "year"). In recent loanwords, it follows more or less the respective languages' pronunciations.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         l
        </b>
        is always pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [l]
        </span>
        , never
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         *[ɫ]
        </span>
        (the English "
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_L" title="Dark L">
         dark L
        </a>
        ").
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         q
        </b>
        only exists in combination with
        <b>
         u
        </b>
        and is pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [kv]
        </span>
        . It appears in both Germanic and Latin words (
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          quer
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [kveːɐ̯]
        </span>
        ;
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Qualität
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [kvaliˈtɛːt]
        </span>
        ). But as most words containing q are Latinate, the letter is considerably rarer in German than it is in English.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         r
        </b>
        is usually pronounced in a
        <a href="/wiki/Guttural_R" title="Guttural R">
         guttural
        </a>
        fashion (a
        <a href="/wiki/Voiced_uvular_fricative" title="Voiced uvular fricative">
         voiced uvular fricative
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʁ]
        </span>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Uvular_trill" title="Uvular trill">
         uvular trill
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʀ]
        </span>
        ) in front of a vowel or consonant (
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Rasen
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈʁaːzən]
        </span>
        ;
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Burg
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [buʁk]
        </span>
        ). In spoken German, however, it is commonly vocalised after a vowel (
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          er
         </span>
        </i>
        being pronounced rather like
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈɛɐ]
        </span>
        –
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Burg
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [buɐk]
        </span>
        ). In some varieties, the
        <b>
         r
        </b>
        is pronounced as a "tongue-tip"
        <i>
         r
        </i>
        (the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
         alveolar trill
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [r]
        </span>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         s
        </b>
        in German is pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [z]
        </span>
        (as in "
        <i>
         z
        </i>
        ebra") if it forms the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syllable_onset" title="Syllable onset">
         syllable onset
        </a>
        (e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Sohn
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [zoːn]
        </span>
        ), otherwise
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [s]
        </span>
        (e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Bus
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [bʊs]
        </span>
        ). In Austria and Switzerland and often in Southern Germany, it is always pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [s]
        </span>
        . A
        <b>
         ss
        </b>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [s]
        </span>
        indicates that the preceding vowel is short.
        <b>
         st
        </b>
        and
        <b>
         sp
        </b>
        at the beginning of words of German origin are pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʃt]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʃp]
        </span>
        , respectively.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         ß
        </b>
        (a letter unique to German called
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          scharfes S
         </span>
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          <a href="/wiki/%C3%9F" title="ß">
           Eszett
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        ) was a ligature of a double
        <b>
         s
        </b>
        <i>
         and
        </i>
        of an
        <b>
         sz
        </b>
        and is always pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [s]
        </span>
        . Originating in
        <a href="/wiki/Blackletter" title="Blackletter">
         Blackletter
        </a>
        typeface, it traditionally replaced
        <b>
         ss
        </b>
        at the end of a syllable (e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          ich muss
         </span>
        </i>
        →
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          ich muß
         </span>
        </i>
        ;
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          ich müsste
         </span>
        </i>
        →
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          ich müßte
         </span>
        </i>
        ); within a word it contrasts with
        <b>
         ss
        </b>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [s]
        </span>
        in indicating that the preceding vowel is long (compare
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          in Maßen
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [in ˈmaːsən]
        </span>
        "with moderation" and
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          in Massen
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [in ˈmasən]
        </span>
        "in loads"). The use of
        <b>
         ß
        </b>
        has recently been limited by the latest German spelling reform and is no longer used for
        <b>
         ss
        </b>
        after a short vowel (e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          ich muß
         </span>
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          ich müßte
         </span>
        </i>
        were always pronounced with a short U/Ü); Switzerland and Liechtenstein already abolished it in 1934.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
         <a href="#cite_note-89">
          [86]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         sch
        </b>
        is pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʃ]
        </span>
        (like "sh" in "shine").
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         tion
        </b>
        in Latin loanwords is pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [tsion]
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         th
        </b>
        is found, rarely, in loanwords and is pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [t]
        </span>
        if the loanword is from Greek, and usually as in the original if the loanword is from English (though some, mostly older, speakers tend to replace the English th-sound with
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [s]
        </span>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         v
        </b>
        is pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [f]
        </span>
        in a limited number of words of Germanic origin, such as
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Vater
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈfaːtɐ]
        </span>
        ,
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Vogel
         </span>
        </i>
        "bird",
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          von
         </span>
        </i>
        "from, of",
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          vor
         </span>
        </i>
        "before, in front of",
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          voll
         </span>
        </i>
        "full" and the prefix
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          ver-
         </span>
        </i>
        . It is also used in loanwords, where it is normally pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [v]
        </span>
        . This pronunciation is common in words like
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Vase
         </span>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Vikar
         </span>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Viktor
         </span>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Viper
         </span>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Ventil
         </span>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          vulgär
         </span>
        </i>
        , and English loanwords; however, pronunciation is
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [f]
        </span>
        by some people in some in the very south. The only non-German word in which "v" is always pronounced "f" is
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Eva
         </span>
        </i>
        (Eve).
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         w
        </b>
        is pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [v]
        </span>
        as in "
        <i>
         v
        </i>
        acation" (e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          was
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [vas]
        </span>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         y
        </b>
        is pronounced as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [y]
        </span>
        when long, and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʏ]
        </span>
        when short (as in
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Hygiene
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [hyɡi̯ˈeːnə]
        </span>
        ;
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Labyrinth
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [labyˈʁɪnt]
        </span>
        or
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          Gymnasium
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɡʏmˈnaːziʊm/
        </span>
        ), except in
        <i>
         ay
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         ey
        </i>
        which are both pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ai]
        </span>
        . It is also often used in loanwords and pronounced like in the original language like in
        <i>
         Style
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         Recycling
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         z
        </b>
        is always pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [t͡s]
        </span>
        (e.g.
        <i>
         <span lang="de">
          zog
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [t͡soːk]
        </span>
        ), except in loanwords. A
        <b>
         tz
        </b>
        indicates that the preceding vowel is short.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Consonant_shifts">
        Consonant shifts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Consonant shifts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For more details on this topic, see
       <a href="/wiki/High_German_consonant_shift" title="High German consonant shift">
        High German consonant shift
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       German does not have any
       <a href="/wiki/Dental_fricative" title="Dental fricative">
        dental fricatives
       </a>
       (as English
       <b>
        th
       </b>
       ). The
       <b>
        th
       </b>
       sounds, which the English language still has, disappeared on the continent in German with the consonant shifts between the 8th and the 10th centuries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is sometimes possible to find parallels between English and German by replacing the English
       <b>
        th
       </b>
       with
       <b>
        d
       </b>
       in German: "Thank" → in German
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Dank
        </span>
       </i>
       , "this" and "that" →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         dies
        </span>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         das
        </span>
       </i>
       , "
       <a href="/wiki/Thou" title="Thou">
        thou
       </a>
       " (old 2nd person singular pronoun) →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         du
        </span>
       </i>
       , "think" →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         denken
        </span>
       </i>
       , "thirsty" →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         durstig
        </span>
       </i>
       and many other examples.
      </p>
      <p>
       Likewise, the
       <b>
        gh
       </b>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic
       </a>
       English words, pronounced in several different ways in modern English (as an
       <b>
        f
       </b>
       , or not at all), can often be linked to German
       <b>
        ch
       </b>
       : "to laugh" →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         lachen
        </span>
       </i>
       , "through" and "thorough" →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         durch
        </span>
       </i>
       , "high" →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         hoch
        </span>
       </i>
       , "naught" →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         nichts
        </span>
       </i>
       , "light" →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         leicht
        </span>
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Licht
        </span>
       </i>
       , "sight" →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Sicht
        </span>
       </i>
       , "daughter" →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Tochter
        </span>
       </i>
       , "neighbour" →
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Nachbar
        </span>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">
        Literature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Literature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/German_literature" title="German literature">
        German literature
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The German language is used in German literature and can be traced back to the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
        Middle Ages
       </a>
       , with the most notable authors of the period being
       <a href="/wiki/Walther_von_der_Vogelweide" title="Walther von der Vogelweide">
        Walther von der Vogelweide
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wolfram_von_Eschenbach" title="Wolfram von Eschenbach">
        Wolfram von Eschenbach
       </a>
       . The
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         <a href="/wiki/Nibelungenlied" title="Nibelungenlied">
          Nibelungenlied
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       , whose author remains unknown, is also an important work of the epoch. The fairy tales collections collected and published by
       <a href="/wiki/Brothers_Grimm" title="Brothers Grimm">
        Jacob and Wilhelm Grimm
       </a>
       in the 19th century became famous throughout the world.
      </p>
      <p>
       Reformer and theologian
       <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">
        Martin Luther
       </a>
       , who was the first to translate the Bible into German, is widely credited for having set the basis for the modern "High German" language. Among the most well known poets and authors in German are
       <a href="/wiki/Gotthold_Ephraim_Lessing" title="Gotthold Ephraim Lessing">
        Lessing
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">
        Goethe
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">
        Schiller
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_von_Kleist" title="Heinrich von Kleist">
        Kleist
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E.T.A._Hoffmann" title="E.T.A. Hoffmann">
        Hoffmann
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bertolt_Brecht" title="Bertolt Brecht">
        Brecht
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Heine" title="Heinrich Heine">
        Heine
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka">
        Kafka
       </a>
       . Thirteen German-speaking people have won the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_literature" title="Nobel Prize in literature">
        Nobel Prize in literature
       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Mommsen" title="Theodor Mommsen">
        Theodor Mommsen
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Christoph_Eucken" title="Rudolf Christoph Eucken">
        Rudolf Christoph Eucken
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_von_Heyse" title="Paul von Heyse">
        Paul von Heyse
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Gerhart_Hauptmann" title="Gerhart Hauptmann">
        Gerhart Hauptmann
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Carl_Spitteler" title="Carl Spitteler">
        Carl Spitteler
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">
        Thomas Mann
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nelly_Sachs" title="Nelly Sachs">
        Nelly Sachs
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Hesse" title="Hermann Hesse">
        Hermann Hesse
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_B%C3%B6ll" title="Heinrich Böll">
        Heinrich Böll
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Elias_Canetti" title="Elias Canetti">
        Elias Canetti
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnter_Grass" title="Günter Grass">
        Günter Grass
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Elfriede_Jelinek" title="Elfriede Jelinek">
        Elfriede Jelinek
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Herta_M%C3%BCller" title="Herta Müller">
        Herta Müller
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="align:center;">
       <tr>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">

         </a>

          (1749–1832)
         </small>
        </th>

        </th>

         </a>
        </td>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="German_loanwords_in_the_English_language">
        German loanwords in the English language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: German loanwords in the English language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       English has taken many
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loanwords
       </a>
       from German, often without any change of spelling (aside from, often, the elimination of umlauts and not capitalizing nouns):
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Organisations">
        Organisations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Organisations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The use and learning of the German language are promoted by a number of organisations.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Goethe-Institut">
        Goethe-Institut
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Goethe-Institut">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Goethe-Institut" title="Goethe-Institut">
        Goethe-Institut
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goethe-Institut_logo.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="150" data-file-width="425" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/37/Goethe-Institut_logo.svg/220px-Goethe-Institut_logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/37/Goethe-Institut_logo.svg/330px-Goethe-Institut_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/37/Goethe-Institut_logo.svg/440px-Goethe-Institut_logo.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goethe-Institut_logo.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Goethe-Institut logo
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The government-backed
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Goethe-Institut
        </span>
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (named after the famous German author
       <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">
        Johann Wolfgang von Goethe
       </a>
       ) aims to enhance the knowledge of German culture and language within Europe and the rest of the world. This is done by holding exhibitions and conferences with German-related themes, and providing training and guidance in the learning and use of the German language. For example, the
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Goethe-Institut
        </span>
       </i>
       teaches the
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Goethe-Zertifikat
        </span>
       </i>
       German language qualification.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Verein_Deutsche_Sprache">
        Verein Deutsche Sprache
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Verein Deutsche Sprache">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Dortmund-based
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Verein Deutsche Sprache (VDS)
        </span>
       </i>
       , which was founded in 1997, supports the German language and is the largest language association of citizens in the world. The VDS has more than thirty-five thousand members in over seventy countries. Its founder, statistics professor Dr. Walter Krämer, has remained chairperson of the association from its beginnings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Deutsche_Welle">
        Deutsche Welle
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Deutsche Welle">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Welle" title="Deutsche Welle">
        Deutsche Welle
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deutsche_Welle_Logo.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="743" height="59" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Deutsche_Welle_Logo.svg/220px-Deutsche_Welle_Logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Deutsche_Welle_Logo.svg/330px-Deutsche_Welle_Logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Deutsche_Welle_Logo.svg/440px-Deutsche_Welle_Logo.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deutsche_Welle_Logo.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Deutsche Welle logo
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The German state broadcaster
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Deutsche Welle
        </span>
       </i>
       is the equivalent of the British
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_World_Service" title="BBC World Service">
        BBC World Service
       </a>
       and provides radio and television broadcasts in German and 30 other languages across the globe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its German language services are tailored for German language learners by being spoken at slow speed.
       <i>
        <span lang="de">
         Deutsche Welle
        </span>
       </i>
       also provides an
       <span class="nowrap">
        e-learning
       </span>
       website to learn German.
      </p>

           <a href="#cite_ref-84">

         <span class="reference-text">
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Zur Definition und sprachwissenschaftlichen Abgrenzung insbesondere
           </span>

          : Rudolf Muhr, Richard Schrodt, Peter Wiesinger (Hrsg.):
          <a class="external text" href="http://www-oedt.kfunigraz.ac.at/OEDTBIB/120-1995-OEDTSBd2.pdf" rel="nofollow">

           </i>
           (PDF, 407 Seiten; 1,3 MB)

          <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140514090855/http://www-oedt.kfunigraz.ac.at/OEDTBIB/120-1995-OEDTSBd2.pdf" rel="nofollow">

          ., Verlag Hölder-Pichler-Tempsky, Wien 1995. Anm.:
          <span lang="de">
           Diese Publikation entstand aus den Beiträgen der Tagung
           <i>
            „Österreichisches Deutsch“
           </i>
           , die mit internationalen Sprachwissenschaftlern an der
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karl-Franzens-Universit%C3%A4t_Graz" title="Karl-Franzens-Universität Graz">

           </a>
           vom 22. bis 24. Mai 1995 stattfand

         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-85">
            ^

          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation web">
           Ha, Thu-Huong.
           <a class="external text" href="https://qz.com/1033265/germanys-century-long-debate-over-a-missing-letter-in-its-alphabet" rel="nofollow">
            "Germany has ended a century-long debate over a missing letter in its alphabet"
           </a>
           <span class="reference-accessdate">
            . Retrieved
            <span class="nowrap">
             5 December
            </span>
            2017
           </span>
           .
           <q>
            According to the council’s 2017 spelling manual: When writing the uppercase [of ß], write SS. It’s also possible to use the uppercase ẞ. Example: Straße — STRASSE — STRAẞE.
           </q>
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Germany+has+ended+a+century-long+debate+over+a+missing+letter+in+its+alphabet&amp;rft.aulast=Ha&amp;rft.aufirst=Thu-Huong&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fqz.com%2F1033265%2Fgermanys-century-long-debate-over-a-missing-letter-in-its-alphabet&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGerman+language">

         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

           <a href="#cite_ref-86">

         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Adolf Reinecke,
          <i>
           <span lang="de">
            Die deutsche Buchstabenschrift: ihre Entstehung und Entwicklung, ihre Zweckmäßigkeit und völkische Bedeutung

        <li id="cite_note-87">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-87">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.ligaturix.de/bormann.htm" rel="nofollow">
           Facsimile of Bormann's Memorandum (in German)
          </a>
          <br/>
          The memorandum itself is typed in Antiqua, but the
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSDAP" title="NSDAP">
           NSDAP
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Letterhead" title="Letterhead">
           letterhead
          </a>
          is printed in Fraktur.
          <br/>
          "For general attention, on behalf of the Führer, I make the following announcement:
          <br/>
          It is wrong to regard or to describe the so-called Gothic script as a German script. In reality, the so-called Gothic script consists of Schwabach Jew letters. Just as they later took control of the newspapers, upon the introduction of printing the Jews residing in Germany took control of the printing presses and thus in Germany the Schwabach Jew letters were forcefully introduced.
          <br/>
          Today the Führer, talking with Herr Reichsleiter Amann and Herr Book Publisher Adolf Müller, has decided that in the future the Antiqua script is to be described as normal script. All printed materials are to be gradually converted to this normal script. As soon as is feasible in terms of textbooks, only the normal script will be taught in village and state schools.
          <br/>
          The use of the Schwabach Jew letters by officials will in future cease; appointment certifications for functionaries, street signs, and so forth will in future be produced only in normal script.
          <br/>
          On behalf of the Führer, Herr Reichsleiter Amann will in future convert those newspapers and periodicals that already have foreign distribution, or whose foreign distribution is desired, to normal script".
         </span>
        </li>

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Germanic+Languages&amp;rft.series=Cambridge+Language+Surveys&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1017%2FCBO9780511755071&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-521-01511-0&amp;rft.aulast=Harbert&amp;rft.aufirst=Wayne&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Febooks.cambridge.org%2Febook.jsf%3Fbid%3DCBO9780511755071&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGerman+language">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li>
         <cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFKönigvan_der_Auwera1994">
          König, Ekkehard; van der Auwera, Johan, eds. (1994).
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.routledge.com/books/details/9780415280792/" rel="nofollow">
           <i>
            The Germanic Languages
           </i>
          </a>
          . Routledge Language Family Descriptions. Routledge.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-415-28079-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-415-28079-2">
           978-0-415-28079-2
          </a>
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            26 February
           </span>
           2015
          </span>
          .
          <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/4176538" rel="nofollow">
           Lay summary
          </a>
          (26 February 2015).
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Germanic+Languages&amp;rft.series=Routledge+Language+Family+Descriptions&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=1994&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-415-28079-2&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.routledge.com%2Fbooks%2Fdetails%2F9780415280792%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGerman+language">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
         The survey of the Germanic branch languages includes chapters by Winfred P. Lehmann, Ans van Kemenade, John Ole Askedal, Erik Andersson, Neil Jacobs, Silke Van Ness, and Suzanne Romaine.
        </li>
        <li>
         W.B. Lockwood,
         <i>
          German Today: The Advanced Learner's Guide
         </i>
         (1987)
         <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
          ISBN
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-19-815850-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-19-815850-5">
          0-19-815850-5
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         Ruth H. Sanders.
         <i>
          German: Biography of a Language
         </i>
         (Oxford University Press; 2010) 240 pages. Combines linguistic, anthropological, and historical perspectives in a "biography" of German in terms of six "signal events" over millennia, including the Battle of Kalkriese, which blocked the spread of Latin-based language north.
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
